“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
Strikes, Labor Questions, and other Econo-
mic difficulties (London : Bliss, Sands and
Foster) has an awkward and misleading
title. The book is really a compact little
treatise on political economy ; and the
author (Mr. A. W. Johnston, of Sydney)
may be heartily congratulated on his per-
formance. Of course, many of his theories
are open to argument (he has not even
commenced to understand the blessings of
Protection) ; of course, he is forced to
dogmatise, for on such a subject every sen-
tence is a text for, a book ten times the
size of his modest Bvo. ; of course, he is
indebted for the bulk of his positions and
arguments to other thinkers. But his
compilation and redaction are so admir-
ably done ; he is so clear, so strong, so
pregnant, and (within his limits) so
thorough, that he can hardly be praised
too highly. Such seed dropped into the
swelling furrow of an enquiring mind will
produce a harvest whose value it is im-
possible to exaggerate. Mr. Johnston’s
cardinal principle is the single-tax on
unimproved land-values, and his state-
ment of it is solid and firm. The extra
chapters on “Imperial Federation” and
“ Australian Credit ” are an anti-climax to
the main argument, and should have been
thrown into the appendix. The book is
strongly recommended to everybody who
has a thinker of his own, and is anxious to
do his own thinking. Cloth, 2s. lOd.
The Mystery of Cioomber, by A. Conan
Doyle (paper, Is. 3d.), is-an unequivocal
surrender to the literary Demos. Not so
long ago, Conan Doyle was going to write
no more detective stories, in the manner
of “Sherlock Holmes,” but devote him-
self entirely to the historical novel for
which he recognised (mistakenly) that his
talent craved. Now he is back again in the
old groove. “The Mystery of Cioomber ”
is only a “Sherlock Holmes” yarn padded
into a small book, with the climax at
the end of 100 pages instead of at the
end of 10. There is certainly a thrill in .
the story, but not enough to justify the
elongation. As in “ The Sign of Four,”
an impending sword terrifies the central
figure; and this time it turns out to be
the sword of Theosophy, which eventually
settles the hash of a wicked General who
has slain a Mahatma. Doyle has a neat
journalistic knack of turning current topics
to the account of his shekel-bag.
How- it Can be Done (cloth, 2s. lOd.) is an
attempt to reply to the constant complaint
of critics of socialism, that it is easy to
pull the old order to pieces, but not easy
to produce a workable scheme for creating
the necessary new era. John Richardson
has produced a book interesting enough,
but not at all helpful in the main difficulty
of socialism-construction which he pro-
fesses to wrestle with and throw. Objec-
tions simply bristle against every step of
his process.
Some Books Worth Reading.
In the Midst of Life, by Ambrose Bierce.
Boards, 2s 6d. Some wonderfully fine
short stories.
The White Company, by Conan Doyle,
Paper, 2s lOd. A good historical novel.
Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle. Paper, 2s lOd.
Adventures of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle. Paper, 2s lOd.

2s IQd. First-class detective stories.

Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is. The author's
best book, well printed.

Reade. Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is.

Paper, 2s lOd. A masterpiece of English
fiction.
Crime and Punishment, by Fedor Dostoieff-
sky. Paper, 3s. One of the most re-
markable of Russian realistic novels.
The Anarchist. Peril, by Felix Dubois.
Cloth, 6s 6d. A startling exposure.
Lorna Doone, by R. D. Blackmore. Cloth,
3s. A charming English romance.

Paper, Is 3d. The bane or the antidote.
Pictures of the Socialistic Future, by Eugene
Richter. Paper, Is 3d. The antidote or
the bane. •

Paper, Is. 3d. Delightfully cynical.
The Green Carnation. Paper, 45.; cloth,
6s, 6d. Witty and amusing.
Virginibus Puerisqqe, by R. L. Stevenson.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. Essays perennially charm-
ing.
My Lady Nicotine, by J. M. Barrie. Cloth,
6s. 6d. A book chief "among ton thousand
and altogether lovely.
In a Canadian Canoe, by Barry Pain. Cloth,
4s. Sketches, verses and stories varying
in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.

Cloth, 6s. 6d. A recent book of idyllic
essays.

The Autocrat of the Breakfast Table. The

A really fine novel, epic in breadth and

Wicked Woman Books.

many points the best of the batch.

element prominent.

very Newest Woman.

Subtly, shamelessly audacious.

Cloth, 4s 3d. A book about wicked women
by a wicked man ; smart and lively, and
full of naughty little thrills.
The Story of a Modern Woman, by Ella
Hepworth Dixon. An insidiously vicious
attack on modern men. Cloth, 4s.
Discords, by George Egerton. This is a
companion volume to “Keynotes,” and
unquestionably strong.

however stubborn and chronic, and the proprietors are

A BIG BIZ!
Cur Trade is Big!, Our Store is Big I
Our Ideas are Bigl
We get bigger every day ; on Saturday night
we are bigger than on the previous Monday,
and on the Wednesday following we are bigger
still. We can’t help growing. We have all the
elements of growth. The soil we are planted in
is good, the climate propitious, the people kind.
We are continually watered by the lifegiving
showers of a nation’s approval. Can anyone
wonder that we grow?
Our growth is a wonderful thing. People
describe it as magical. There is no magic in it.
Anyone, or any set of people, can do the same if
they do as we have done. But they don’t; they
can’t find the way, charge too much for the privi-
lege of doing business with them, and generally
and deplorably make a mess of the whole affair.
That’s the mischief.

“The Bulletin’s” Scrap-Book .
SONG-WORDS.
Turning the leaves in an idle way
Of the book I was skimming the other day,
I found a line at the end of a song,
Which keeps on haunting me all day long
With its sweet and mournful melody—
“ Oh, Jane, my love, had you loved but me !”
Sadder a burden could never be
Than, “Jane, my love, had you loved but me!”
Few words and simple—but, O, how much
The singer has told in that little touch !
How hard a story of chances lost,
Of bright hopes blighted and true love crossed
Is heard in the whispered melody,
“ Oh, Jane, my love, had you loved but me !’’
To many a sorrow the key may be ;
“ Ob, Jane, my love, had you loved but me !”
I don’t believe in what poets have said
Of hearts that are broken and lives that are dead ;
Lives well-ordered will stand to their course,
And hearts of true metal ring little the worse;
But they vibrate still to that melody,
“ Oh, Jane, my love, had you loved but me !
My life is well, but what would it be,
Sweet Jane, my love, had you loved but me !”
The world rolls on and years roll by,
Day-dreams vanish and memories die;
But it surges up with a restless pain,
That long-lost longing over again.
Breathed in the passionate melody,
“ Oh, Jane, my love, had you loved but me,
My life is well, but what would it be,
Sweet Jane, my love, had you loved but me?’
TWO WOMEN.
The shadows lay along Broadway,
’Twas near the twilight-tide—
And slowly there a lady fair
Was walking in her pride ;
Alone walked she; but, viewlessly,
Walked spirits at her side.
Peace charmed the street beneath her feet,
And Honor charmed the air;
And all astir looked kind on her,
And called her good as fair—
For all God ever gave to her,
She kept with chary care.
She kept with care her beauties rare
From lovers warm and true,
For her heart was cold to all but gold,
And the rich came not to woo —
But honored well are charms to sell
If priests the selling do.
Now, walking there was one more fair—
A slight girl, lily-pale ;
And she had unseen company
To make the spirit quail :
’Twixt Want and Scorn she walked forlorn,
And nothing could avail.
No mercy now can clear her brow
For this world’s peace to pray ;
For. as love’s wild prayer dissolved in air,
Her woman’s heart gave way !
But the sin forgiven lay Christ in heaven
By man is cursed alway !
NATHANIEL P. WILLIS.
LEONAINIE.
(After Edgar Allan Poe.)
Leonainie, angels called her,
And they took the light
Of the laughing stars and trained her
In a smile qf white.
And they made her hair of the gloomy
Midnight, and her eyes of the bloomy
Moonshinej and they brought her to me
In a solemn night.
In a solemn night of summer
When my heart of gloom
Blossomed up to meet the comer,
Like a rose in bloom ;
Then all feelings that oppressed me
Left me there, as joy caressed me—
Lying joy, that caught and pressed me
In the arms of doom.
Then spake the little lisper
In the angel tongue—
Yet, I, listening, heard her whisper—
Songs are only sung
Here below that they may grieve you,
Tales are told you' to deceive you,
So must Leonainie leave you
While her love is young.
Then God smiled and it was morning,
Matchless and supreme,
Heaven’s glory seemed adorning
All earth with its esteem.
Every heart but mine seemed gifted
With the voice of prayer, and lifted
Where my Leonainie drifted
From me like a dream.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling —with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stain « of airy Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney . In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfei 'c
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other A rticles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Itluck and White.
Anyone supplying loThe Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and inte est and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for s line on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under

SATURDAY, xMAY 4, 1895.
A Lag-Time Legend.
m tv i i n i 4 a Tttn
The Disorderly Conduct Suppression Bill,
successfully piloted through the JSew boutli
Wales Council by Mr. R. E. O’Connor,
M.L.A., is another proof that the province
has not forgotten the w r ays of its lag-time
forefathers. Not that the proof is seriously
, , m . t
needed. The country is still very new its
ancient history is but a thing of yesterday,
and there still survive patriarchs from among
the people of the old, gory regime to. keep
its memory putrid in the laud. But Mr.
O’Connor’s bill, and his supporters in the
Upper House, and the one or two papers
which dare to enthuse over them, all help to
remind us that the convict epoch is nearer
than it seems. The history of the New
South Wales criminal code, from its com-
mencement down to the present day, has
been short aud simple. It began with the
cat and the gallows. It developed'mto more
cat and more gallows. Then an epoch of
Botany Bay humanitariauism—which is
wholly different from every other kind of
humanitariauism—set in, and there was cat
aud a little less gallows. 1 hereafter, for a
number of years, there were cat and gallows
in varying quantities and degrees,and now the
Lag. Council has decided that what the couu-
try needs is more cat. In the beginning the
morals of the population were generally bad,
and the cat was prescribed for their improve-
ment. They grew worse, and more cat was
prescribed to arrest theii decline. limy
grew no better, and a little less cat was tried,
to see if that would effect any change.
Thereafter they improved considerably, and
the aggregate foolishness uf the Upper
House recognises the improvement with
joy, and feels that the only thing required
to make it permanent and cont inuous is
more cat. Its utter inability to recognise
any possible remedy for a mind diseased
save the cat is stupid, pitiable, abject—but
then the Upper House is all these things by
nature, and swinish and ignorant to boot.
* • #
Mr. R. E. O’Connor’s bill is noteworthy
as being, in some respects, about the best
example of the Council’s innate callousness
which has appeared for a good many years,
In other respects it is a well-meaning but
stupid measure, which only escapes being
commend .ble because the draughtsman could
not got out of the old groove or forget the
old precedents. Its main provision is con
tained in clause 5, which enacts that any
male above the age of 18 (the prescribed
was at first twelve years) who commits a
violent and unprovoked assault may bo sen-
tenced, at the discretion of one magistrate,
to imprisonment with hai’d labor for 12
months and three floggings of 30 lashes each.
Hitherto it has required two magistrates to
order the lash, and even then their powers
in this direction were comparatively limited.
Thus a certain semblance of decency was
kept up, for it seldom happened that the two
stipendiaries were drunk at the same time,
or, if they were, it often chanced that only
one was a bloodthirsty drunk. It is a suffi-
ciently well-known fact that many magis-
trates are alcoholised, and many more know
very little law, and have tempers that are
aggravated by various inflammatory diseases,
and that they give strangely unaccountable
decisions, and are commonly addicted to
rushing through ninety cases in sixty minutes,
in consequence of which the prisoner’s de-
fence generally has to go unheard. But,
with all, the fact that two magistrates acting
conjointly are necessary before the most
horrible penalties of Botany Bay law can
be inflicted without the intervention of a
Supreme Court Judge and jury, has helped
to preserve some faint semblance of decency.
Mr. O’Connor’s Bill is apparently intended to
do away with all that. When it becomes law,
the magistrate, whoever he may be, who holds
the record for velocity, while he is getting
through ninety cases in sixty minutes, may
stop just half-a-second on case No. 47 to
order twelve months and three floggings,
and then be well on with case No. 62 before
the prisoner is fairly removed out of the
Court. Or the drunken magistrate, or the
bloodshot stipendiary with the corrugated
liver, may do the same between two hic-
cups. The worst and most ferocious punish-
ment in all the criminal co le is to be put
within the jurisdiction of the lowest, worst-
paid, least educated, most insignificant indi-
vidual on the Bench. After this the only
thing required to round off the measure and
make it absolutely complete, is a proviso that
the death penalty may be inflicted by the
back-blocks magistrate, or by Patrick
O’Booligan Mac Mullins, Esq., the bush
J.P.
» • *
The other clauses provide that any person
convicted of riotous or indecent conduct
shall be liable to a fine of £2O, with the
alternative of six months’ gaol for the first
offence, to six months “ without the option”
for the second, and to twelve months for the
third ; that any person convicted of either of
the above offences, with wilful injury to
property in addition, shall be liable for the
first offence to a fine of £2O with the alterna-
tive of twelve months, and to makegood the
damage, and for the second offence to twelve
months without the option ; and that any
person who adds the throwing of road-metal
to riot or indecency shall be liable to nine
months’ imprisonment if alone, or twelve
months if acting in collusion with others.
All these penalties are fully justifiable, if
they are likely to be operative, but as they
are not they are mere fooolishness. There
is already abundance of law for the sup-
pression of riot, assault and similar offences,
but in 99 cases out of 109 a weak-kneed and
morally groggy magistrate lets the murderous-
assault man off with a fine which varies from
20s. to £lO. In the remaining case two
ferocious “beaks’ probably order imprison-
ment and a tremendous flogging. The exist-
ing law says that the magistrate “ may
order the brutal larrikin a long term of hard
labor, but he does nothing of the kind. The
new law says that the same mentally groggy
magistrate “may” order a longer term of
imprisonment, but the man who lacks the
courage or the sense to enforce one law will
fail equally to enforce the other Because
the present law is a dead letter, larr kinism
flourishes. And beoauselarrikinism flourishes,
R. E. O’Connor and the hoggish Legislative
Council propose to give to the one ferocious
magistrate who can be found here and there
amid a crowd of fools and weaklings, power
to inflict, at his own sole discretion, horrible
tortures upon one casual criminal where a
thousand others escape.
# # *
If the proposed Bill gets on the Statute-
Book, to be an eternal disgrace to the people
of New South Wales, the results will be very
much as foffows ;—There will still be not
half, or nearly half, enough police and detec-
tives to capture criminals, therefore the same
large proportion will escape as heretofore.
The weakness of the force will make it diffi-
cult, just as it always- has been, to find the
right criminal, even when an arrest is made,
therefore the tendency to capture somebody,
very much at random, and push a conviction
against him on insufficient evidence, will
still remain. The same foolish and
weak kneed magistrate, who now fines
the street ruffian 295. when the law says he
“may” be sentenced to six months’ hard
labor will continue to fine him 20s. when
R. E. O’Connor’s new law says he “ may ”
be sentenced to 12 months, with or without
three floggings. But in two or three places
there will arise two or three butcher-like
stipendiaries, with a ghoulish love for the
cat and the triangles, who will sentence
prisoners freely to the utmost horrors of the
law. And, as the stipendiary butcher will
only get his own very small share of really
hard cases, lie will gratify his gory instincts
by inflicting these sentences on comparatively
minor offenders. Therefore, if the man who
comes within an ace of actual murder is
brought up before any one of the great mul-
titude of feeble and incapable magistrates,
he will be fined the same old 20s. or £5 ;
and if the man who is charged with a com-
paratively trifling assault, on the evidence,
possibly, of a very dubious policeman, is
brought before a magistrate of the other
description, he stands a fair chance of being
sentenced to 12 months and 90 lashes. The
evil of the present system is that the
police court law is a gamble, where the
heaviest sentence goes to the greatest
or the least offender indiscriminately,
and where the magistrate has so much “ dis-
cretion ” that he can (and generally does)
fine the genteel offender in a tall silk hat in
the sum of a few shillings, and at the same
time can (and often does) visit the man in
moleskins and blucher-boots with three
months for an exactly similar offence. And
Mr. O Connor’s bill, which says simply that
the perp strator of an aggravated assault
“ may ” be flogged or imprisoned, or both,
leaves the magistrate power to flog or im-
prison the workman, and fine the tall-hatted
criminal a few shillings as heretofore. If an
equally scandalous latitude were granted in
the Supreme Court, then the penalty for
wilful murder would be anything from £5
fine up to the gallows, and the Judge could
inflict either punishment according to his
liver, or the social status of the murderer.
* « •
A measure for the suppression of dis-
orderly conduct, riot, brutal assault, and
the like, is wanted in New South Wales
more than almost any other kind of crimi-
nal legislation. But, if it is to be
effective, it requires to be built on very
different principles from the bill of Mr.
O’Connor, M.L.G. The first essential is to
largely strengthen the police force, which
now consists of one man to every 175 square
miles—to double it at the very least, and
possibly to treble it ; also, to greatly increase
the detective force, and raise the pay of the
officers to a figure at which they can all
afford to be passably honest, and at which
none of them will be absolutely compelled
to eke out their miserable official pittances by
bribes from gambling-houses, brothels, fan-
tan dens, totalisators, and the like. The
expense would be large, but it could easily
be met by charging a halfpenny postage on
newspapers, to quote the simplest and most
equitable out of a dozen possible means of
raising revenue. By this reform there would
be a fair chance of catching the criminal in
the first instance where, in a large propor-
tion of cases, he now escapes altogether.
Further, there would be a better chance of
catching him promptly, and of catching the
right criminal. No amount of bloodthirsti-
ness in the criminal code will do anything
towards suppressing crime unless the offender
can be secured with something like reason-
able certainty to begin with, yet the legis-
lators, whose one idea of reform is to make
the code more and more b irbarous, seem
unable to comprehend this self-evident axiom.
• • •
A second and equally essential reform is
to make punishment certain and reliable
after the offender is captured and oonviote 1.
The present statute allows the police magis-
trate to visit disorderly conduct or aggravated
assault with a long term of hard labor. The
common plea that it has failed to diminish
these offences and that it is necessary to re-
inforce it with additional barbarities is
utterly false ; the existing law has never yet
had a trial. Only the other day, in Sydney,
a crowd of ruffians who nearly murdered a
publican without"- provocation were let off
with aggregate fines of £l5, and this was an
unusual instance of severity ; the average
price of a murderous assault is £5. The kind
of statute that is wanted is one enacting that
the person who commits a savage and unpro-
voked attack upon another shall get six
months hard labor for the first offence, and
an increased sentence for subsequent offences,
without any discretion whatever on the part
of the magistrate. Circumstances have long
since proved that the average “beak ” is not
naturally endowed with discretion, and the
laws efforts to compel him to possess
that valuable quality have utterly failed.
Also, in cases of larrikinism, disor-
derly conduct, wilful destruction of pro-
perty and indecency, a hard and fast
minimum punishment should be decreed, so
that the P.M. shall be compelled to adminis-
ter the law which he now practically ignores.
And, if the Legislature desires to maintain
the cat and triangles as features of the crimi-
nal code, let it enact—not that they “ may ”
be applied for certain offences, at the discre-
tion of the Bench, or according to its liver,
or in a fixed ratio to the clothes of the
offender ; but that they shall be applied to
these offences, so that the criminal in a new
suit and a high collar must stand on the same
plane as the one in moleskins. The system
by which the magistrate or the J.P. is
allowed to enforce the law or ignore it, at his
own sole will and pleasure, is a public dis-
grace and one of the principal causes of
crime, and the Legislature which abolishes
it will do more good to New South Wales
than all the uncivilised lawmakers of the past
century.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Bankruptcy, Composition and
Decomposition.
Now that most of Melbourne’s large and evil-
smelling boomers hive got their compositions
through and have paid one fink, or there-
ab mbs, in the alleged £, the Victorian authori-
ties are thinking about amending their utterly
scandalous insolvency law. As a preliminary
measure, a “ Board ” was recently appointed to
find out what was the matter with the present
system, and this body started out to take evi-
dence. It sat for one day, and in that brief
period it was staggered by such a mass of
ugly revelations that it adjourned for a
week’s rest. Judge Molesworth and Mr.
Jacomb, assignee of the Insolvency Court, were
the two witnesses examined, and the following
are a few of the facts elicited : It is a common
practice for the big and knowing insolvent to
give bills to all his friends for imaginary debts;
these friends then roll up at the meeting, and
either bully the real creditors or outvote them,
and decide to take a composition of, perhaps,
in the £. Solicitors who “ know how to do
it ” bear a hand in this work. In country dis-
tricts, the Registrar is mostly an underpaid
youth of little experience, and “ obvious influ-
ences ” are brought to bear to make him
grant a discharge in cases which won’t
bear inspection. “ Obvious influences,” by the
way, is a term which might include wine, lovely
woman, a knowledge of the feeble youth’s
amours, his unpaid tailor’s bill, hard cash, or
many other circumstances. Where liquidation
is resorted to, the insolvent’s “ friends,” being
the majority, often secure the appointment of
another complaisant friend as trustee, while the
real creditors make unavailing protests. The
friend thus appointed generally contrives to
smother any objectionable person who wants to
inquire into things, and he is often paid, out of
the estate, much more than the amount fixed by
law, especially in a case where much smother-
ing is required. The law requires the trustee
to file quarterly accounts, giving some reason-
able excuse for the disappearance of the
assets supposed to be in his charge, but
he often fails to do this. Sometimes—not
often, though—he is sacked in consequence, but
even then he is only sacked so far as that par-
ticular estate is concerned, and his unreliability
is no bar to his continuing in the business.
The trustee and the solicitor often “hold
examinations in court until the assets are all
gone, the money being practically divided”
between the two of them. The law declares that
a bankrupt can’t get his rele ise on less than a 7s.
in the £ dividend, except in cases of unusual
misfortune; therefore, as Judge Molesworth
remarked, “ it is merely a case of hard swear-
ing.” The man who swears to a series of
astonishing misfortunes gets off; the man
who isn’t good at swearing doesn’t. The often
fraudulent |d. in the £ composition never even
comes before the court in the ordinary way of
business; it is secretly “sanctioned” as a
matter of course by a small clerk on payment
of 20s. Unclaimed dividends are supposed to
be paid into the Treasury, but they are very
often retained by the trustee and never again
heard of. Under the present system “ a
man can make up a fictitious list of creditors
and pay what dividend he pleases.” If half
the allege I debts are fictitious then half the
total dividend is clear profit to somebody. A
list of twenty defaulting trustees some
of them with thousands of pounds un
accounted for was produced, and this
did not profess to be a complete list either. It
was further explained that one of the conditions
of the appointment of a trustee was that he
should halve his commission with the creditors
—often bogus creditors—who voted for his
appointment, and that “all the big men in
Melbourne ” followed this fraudulent practice.
It was just here that the court was overcome
with emotion and felt its legs giving way
beneath it, and consequently it had to adjourn
for a week. The Victorian insolvency Ixw is a
thing which needs to be approached gently, and
stirred up with a long pole from a distance.
When it is stirred up suddenly, at close
quarters, no board can stand the odor.
The N.S.W. Civil Service Commission.
The New South Wales Civil Service Commis-
sion has presented a report which is calculated
to make the aggregate hair of the community
stand on end. It states, among other things,
that, in some of the more gorgeous departments,
the messenger—the man who runs about with
letters and fixes an arrogant eye on visitors—
gets as much as £3OO a year. Many other mes-
sengers get from £125 to £2OO. The individual
who works the lift is paid on the same inflated
scale. There are 7009 temporary officers, many
of whom are quite unnecessary. In many
cases one or two clerks are the sole occupants
of a room, and the waste of space thus involved
makes it necessary to hire much office accoim
modation at high rentals. Many highly-paid
officers are employed solely in pottering over
books which could be kept by any boy of
reasonable intelligence. The audit is often
months in arrear, and, except in the case of the
Customs, there is no personal audit whatever;
the department simply forwards its vouchers
by post, and its books are not even examined.
Consequently one series of frauds went on
for eight years without the large and vastly
expensive Audit Department taking the
slightest notice of the matter. The sei-
vice is full of useless inspectors. In the
words of the report: “The country is abso-
lutely overrun with Government officers trave -
ling ; you cannot go to any town without find-
ing 9 half-a-dozen different inspectors inspecting
water-tanks, police-stations and public build
ings,” &o. This, by the way, takes no account
of Dibbs’ “ inspectors of agriculture.” I* l ®
Master in Equity reports that he has one
accountant for whom he has no earthly use,
and the Statistician complains about an officer
in his department (salary, £470 per annum) toi
whom there is no employment whatever, am
two others whose salaries are in excess of then
value. The expenses in connection with
charitable institutions are reported to be grossly
extravagant. In the Treasury the salaries are
exorbitant, the officials too numerous, and the
work slovenly. In the Customs, much simp o
work which could be done by boys is in the
hands of highly-paid officials ; the landing-
waiter (£334 a year) is superfluous ; and the
costly steam launch is far too expensive and
dignified for its work. TheGovqrnmentPrintulg'
office is reported to be choked up with vast
misses of unsoll and unused literature


ordered for publication by the Assembly ; the
quantity now on hand having cost £26,000 to
produce, and having & saleable value _ of
about £IOO. Some of these volumes, which
nobody ever reads, are bound at a charge of
365. per volume. The Health Board, the
almost useless Marine Board, the pilot-service,
and the quarantine staff are all either over-
manned or overpaid, or both. The Public In-
struction Department, for some unexplained
reason, keeps an architectural staff of its own,
instead of having its work done by the Govern-
ment Architect’s office. The number of Circuit
Courts and Courts of Quarter Sessions is reported
to be excessive, and in small towns the billets
of land agent and C.P.S. might be amalgamated.
The prospecting vote of £20,000 a year is ap-
parently almost absolute waste; out of the
mines assisted from this vote not one in 400
has proved payable. The Examiner of Coal-
fields is a useless excrescence; there is no
more need for him than for an examiner of
granite. Also, there are two clerks (£514 per
annum) attached to the diamond drill. The
rents of mining leases are £14,000 in arrear ;
and the Government Metallurgist (£I3OO a year)
has been drawing his salary for three years, but
has been unable to do any work because no work-
shop or apparatus have been provided. The Post
Dili ;e, the Lan Is, the Education Department,
and most other public offices are in a similar con-
demn of muddle and chaos. Altogether, the
c nnmission points out that £250,000 a year can
be saved, chiefly by abolishing officers who got
their appointments by political influence, and
for whom there is no useful employment what-
ever. A good many other commissions, by the
way, have pointed out the same thing, and the
.only result has been that one batch of useless
officers was sacked, and pensioned or “com-
pensated ” out of the insolvent superannuation-
fund, and the uncles ami cousins and supporters
„of the Ministry appointed in their places.
Faymen; of Members.
The value of the main point in the attempt to
upset Payment of Members in New South
Wales can be judged by every person who reads
and writes English. Here is clause 2 from the
Enabling Act of ’B9 :
Every member of the Legislative Assembly now serving
or hereafter to serve therein shall, unless he is one of the
persons specified or referred to in the next following
section, be entitled to receive by way of reimbursement
for expenses incurred hy him in the discharge of his Par-
liamentary duties an allowance at the rate of t3OO per
annum, which shall he charged on the consolidat'd
revenue fund, and be payable monthly to every in m ier
o: this present Legislative Assembly now serving, from
the date ..f the passing of this Act, and to every member
hereafter elect d from the lime of his taking his seat,
and in every case until he shall resign or his seat be
vacated, or until Parliament shall be dissolved or shall
expire by effluxion of time.
The Consolidated Fat Man alleges that this
clause can only refer to the Legislative Assembly
of the Parliament which passed the Act, “ in-
asmuch as the words ‘ hereafter to serve
therein ’ could only apply to the Legislative
Assembly previously mentioned, and such
Legislative Assembly was defined by the words
‘ now serving therein ’ to be the Legislative
Assembly in existence at the date of the passing
of the Act.” Which doesn’t follow; and the
more you look at it the less it seems to follow.
For look at the whole clause. It divides
itself easily into two patts : one referring
to “ the Legislative Assembly ” in its permanent
aspect, and the other to “ this present Legisla-
tive Assembly ” in its temporary aspect. If
these are identical, why not “ the Legislative
Assembly” throughout ? why lay such emphatic
stress upon “ this present Legislative Assem-
bly?” Simply because there was a contention
at the time that such legislation should only be
prospective, and the Assembly was anxious to
put beyond doubt that its members also were
entitled to reimbursement. Hence, very clearly
and logically, the clause first lays down the
{irinciple of payment as applied to “ the Legis-
ative Assembly”—the Legislative Assembly
recognised as part of the Constitution of the
province—and then makes definite application
of the principle to the members of tlxe Legis-
lative Assembly then sitting. In this
view, the neat verbal obstacle raised by
the O.F.M. is quite off the track of the
■argument. It is a weak obstacle, anyhow.
Suppose that the clause read: “Every mem-
ber of the Parliament of New South Wales now
serving, or hereafter to serve therein, shall,”
&c. Would that refer only to a single Parlia-
ment or to the Parliament of all time ? To the
■latter, decidedly ; especially when “the Parlia-
ment of New Soutli Wales ” was contrasted
with “ this present Parliament of New South
Wales now serving.” The words “ the Legis-
lative Assembly ” can be used in precisely the
same general sense as “ the Parliament of New
South Wales.” The phrase “ hereafter to serve
therein” applies just as well to M.’s.L.A. 500
years hence. They will be just as much members
of “the Legislative Assembly ” as are any who sit
to-day. The Legislative Assembly has a generic,
continuous aspect as well as a special, limited
lispect. It is at the same time a chain and a
link in a chain. Therefore, to mark the differ-
ence, the draughtsman of the Payment of
Members Act refers to it as “ the Legislative
Assembly ” in the large sense, and “ this pre-
sent Legislative Assembly ” in the small sense.
What m ue can he do? Is he to slur over the
fact that “ the Legislative Assembly ” is a part
of and co-exists with a permanent Parliament,
and lay stress only on the fact that “ a Legis-
lative Assembly,” like a Parliament, has a
beginning and end ? We have a succession of
Parliaments, but only one Parliament; a succes-
sion of Legislative Assemblies, but only one
“the Legislative Assembly.” Doubtless, in
d tily newspaper perspective, which magnifies
objects close 'at hand and dwarfs those at a
distance, “ the Legislative Assembly ” means
the particular Legislative Assembly sitting on
the day the paper is published. But an Act of
Parliament is not a daily newspaper, and it
deals, not with 24 hours, but with all time.
And it should be easy to show from the N.S.W.
Parliamentary records that the Payment of
Members Act was certainly looked upon as pro-
viding for future Parliaments; and that the prin-
ciple was all the more contested on that account.
But if the Privy Council—to which the case
is pretty sure to go—should take a view
contrary to this argument, there wJI indeed be
a joyous rattling of legislators whose right
hands are regularly lifted in denunciation of
payment of members, while their left hands as
regularly pouch the pay. The spectacle of a
lot of members—among them the most marvel-
lously-resurrected corpse in Australian politics
—absorbing their monthly cash like dry sponges
while reiterating “ I, Mr. Speaker, am utterly
opposed to payment of members ”—endeavor-
ing to cry on one side of their faces and laugh
on the other, to combine the most sincere private
grief with the most sincere appearance of public
joy—will indeed be an interesting spectacle.
Unfortunately, the country cannot be content
to admire the comedy. If the Privy Council
makes Payment of Members illegal under the
present Act, then a new Act must be passed at
once to legalise it. Willy-nilly, shilly-shally,
Wobbler Reid must be forced to beard the Leg.
Council in its den. Payment of Members is the
keystone of democratic government as at
present conducted. It is a bridge which
spans the gulf between the class legisla-
tion of the past, when only wealthy men
could afford to sit in Parliament and
wdien they naturally made law's in the interest
of wealthy men exclusively, and the broader,
if still imperfect, legislation of the present,
through which the representatives of the
workers voice the workers’ greater needs and
claims. Land Acts and Local Government Acts
are mere excrescences compared with the vital
Payment of Members question. Get a good
representative Parliament and good representa-
tive legislation will follow. But it is useless to
expect a decent superstructure without a sound
foundation.
The Royal Commission Agitation.
Mr. W. A. Walker aspires to instruct the
Dean Salvation Committee as to the law of
New South Wales post-conviction inquiries. As
Premier Reid had, a few days previously,
instructed the Legislative Assembly on the
same subject' the Dean Committee was pro-
bably fully seized of the law thus again worried
by Mr. Walker —section 283 of the Criminal
Law Amendment Act. It is true that this sec-
tion provides for the holding of a further
enquiry by “ any justice,” but it is also true
that the section declares that such “justice”
can only hold such enquiry “ by direction of
the Governor, on the petition of the prisoner, or
some person on his behalf;” or “by direction
of a Judge of the Supreme Court of his own
motion.” Applying this machinery to the
Dean case: “ Governor,” no doubt, means
“ Governor-in-Oouncil,” i.e. , the Executive. If
so, the Executive lias already decided to
bury Dean alive, and it would be waste
of time to petition that body. Assuming,
on the other hand, it means the “ Governor ”
personally, the present occupant of the
vice-regal office is only Lieutenant-Governor,
and is, officially to the community, the Chief
Justice. As Chief Justice, Mr. Darley could
order an enquiry before a J.P., “of his own
motion.” He doesn’t, and so it seems useless
to apply to him. The New South Wales public
is not such a flat as to ask— (a) Windeyer, J.,
to order a further enquiry, after declaring that
he felt as assured of Dean’s guilt as if he had
seen him apply and supply the poison ; or (b)
expect any other Supreme Court Judge, “of
his own motion,” to interfere gratuitously with
one of “ Brother Windeyer’s ” cases. There-
fore, to all practical intents and purposes, so
far as the entombed Dean is concerned, the
283rd section of the Criminal Law Amendment
Act might never have been drafted, and Mr. W.
A. Walker, of “ Chambers,” is vexing his soul
unprofitably, and casting technical instruction
upon the waste places of the community.
♦
Could there be any more convincing illustra-
tion of the national indecency and positive
wickedness of a political Attorney-General-
ship than the recent developments of
the Dean case? That it should be pos-
sible for a man to be appointed Attorney-
General, not merely because he is an honor-
able lawyer of upright judgment, but
because, also, he has been a more or less
foxy politician—is of itself sufficiently bad.
But that an Attorney-General who has been
Crown Prosecutor and special pleader against
a prisoner ; who has, no matter how sincerely,
pronounced his opinion of that prisoner’s guilt;
and who has succeeded in getting that prisoner
sentenced to death, possibly as the result of an
utterly incorrect theory; should afterwards
be called upon and expected to take his seat at
the Executive Council, and, as by far its most
masterful member, help in deciding whether
that prisoner was fairly tried and justly sen-
tenced all this is so revolting to ideas of fair-
play that one is inclined to ask himself
which barbarous nation owns him as a
citizen. With the Dean incidents freshly
in mind, a political Attorney-General be-
comes an emblem of the forgotten horrors of
a Star-Chamber inquisition. There is in New
South Wales no more manly man all round than
Mr. Want, Q.C. ; but wasn’t Mr. Want
Attorney-General at the time of the hideous
Mount Rennie trial ? If so, is he content to
allow the unslaughtered survivors to linger in
prison after such a trial? The Bulletin
invites the attention l of the press and poli-
ticians and public of Australasia to the hours
over which the Mount Rennie trial extended,
and to the subsequent careers of the two Crown
witnesses on whose testimony the case for the
prosecution was chiefly based.
Premier Reid (N.S.W.) states, as definitely as
he ever states anything, that he won’t even
consider the advisability of appointing a Public
Defender. Under the present system, when a
serious crime is committed, the police arrest
the person who, in the opinion of Constable
O’ Hooligan, is the most probable criminal.
Then the police and detectives proceed to work
up evidence agaiist him, and the Crown
Law Department prepares the case, and the
Treasury supplies the needful funds, and
l vo or three able barristers are en-
gaged at great expense to impress upon the
jury, to the utmost of their ability, every point
that can tell against the accused, and to slur
over or discredit or explain away or flatly con-
tradict everything that can tell in his favor.
Meanwhile, the prisoner is locked up in his cell;
he can’t get round to hunt up evidence for the
defence, or to see that what evidence is avail-
able isn’t tampered with or suppressed ; very
often he has no money with which to hire a
solicitor or only enough to engage a cheap and
inefficient one; and he seldom can find the large
sums required to hire private detectives to
collect testimony on his behalf. Also, if he
pays a lawyer, he has no means of making sure
that that lawyer does his duty. Therefore, in
the case of a poor prisoner, only one side of
the story is presented to the jury ; it is only in
the case of a rich prisoner (who can gain his
release on bail in cases where a penniless man
has to stay in gaol) that both aspects of the
affair are fairly put before the court. And
about the only straight and definite statement
which Premier Reid has made during his term
of office is that he won’t even consider the
advisability of doing something to correct this
monstrous injustice.

S.M. Herald pays a grudging compliment to
the Labor-party in New South Wales Parlia-
ment :
From the earliest hour at which the House assem-
bles to the latest at which it rises, there is one section
which is found always to be represented at approximately
its full strength.
Meanwhile, the representatives of Fat are not
nearly so assiduous ; they are frequently absent
on business or pleasure ; and the Herald calls
upon Premier Reid to undertake no important
business till it is convenient for them to attend.
Which is equivalent to an admission that the
Labor-members give the cream of their brains
and time to the service of the country, while
their opponents offer up only the fag-ends which
they have to spare from private affairs. There-
fore, the Herald would penalise—not the idle
members, the laggards and sluggards, but the
industrious ones who recognise that the State
which pays their salaries has first claim on their
attention. Fat logic !
Ed. Bulletin. —At Mildura (Vic.) Trenwith,
M.L.A., said, among other things :
There must be some.hing radically wrong when there
was more wheat, more houses, more merchandise in the
world than ever before, and cheaper than ever before, and
yet more hungry, homeless, and ill-clad people. The in-
troduction of machinery had cheapened production, and
had displaced labor. The problem was to find employ-
ment at living wages for the displaced labor. There was
enough for everybody in the world, but it was badly dis-
tributed. — Arhus report.
Whereupon the jocular man of the Argus pro-
claimed Trenwith as an opponent of machinery
and progress. Trenwith was compared to the
Chinese Mandarin who took this objection to a
proposed local railway:
That the 25,000 men who now gain a living by each
carrying two slung half-hundredweight boxes of tea to
the seaboard would he deprived of work.
Trenwith has a right to know what the Argus
would do about those 25,000 men, anyhow. If
the railway would produce such increased
wealth as to permit compensation being given
to them, then an equitable progress might be
attained. But the progress that would sacri-
fice the living of 25,000 men and their families
to make a railway syndicate’s fortune is so
characteristically Tory as to need no apology.
The Argus has a short memory. On Bth April,
nine days before, it spoke thus :
Their (the Electric Light Oo.’s)grievance possesses none
of the harsh features of the case of the innumerable cab-
drivers who were swept off the city thoroughfares, with-
out a shilling of compensation or an atom of sympathy
from anybody, by the Tramway Co.
The Argus can admit on occasion the anomalies
of its sort of “ progress.” The Socialist has no
grievance against Progress, or any of its ser-
vants, not excluding machinery. It is when
machinery enriches a few by depriving many
that the system which permits such injustice
becomes particularly obnoxious. The question
is, not the prohibition of machinery, but the
equitable distribution of its advantages. Com-
petition, Supply and Demand, the Tory trinity,
professes to keep watch over the “ progress of
the race ” —but it dues not even pretend to care
for the wants of the unit. Socialism bases its
calculations on the unit’s necessities.—F.
Dear Bulletin, — New South Wales sugar-
growers petition Parliament not to remove
the duty from sugar. Their petition
should be accompanied by an undertak-
ing to employ only white labor. The object
of a protective policy is to save the white
Australian from degrading competition with
cheap alien labor. But the New South
Whiles sugar industry thrives partly by the
employment of colored aliens. That is to say,
the white worker has to pay more for his sugar
in order, not that his fellow-worker may be
employed to the general benefit, but in order
that cheap black-trash may be employed at an
exorbitant profit to the general loss. That is not
Protection. Protection should logically pro-
tect just as much against the cheap laborer as
against the product of cheap labor. The sugar
industry, as carried on in Q. and New South
Wales, does not deserve protection. If we
have to consume the products of black labor, it
is Better to get them cheaply and keep the
black laborer in Fiji and Java, than to pay
dearly for them and bring the black laborer
within our own boundaries to compete with the
white worker. A stink abroad is better than a
stink at home. The bonused Australian sugar-
industry is at present almost entirely a colored-
labor industry. It should be compelled to
choose which it will give up —its bonus or its
niggers.—B.B.
The attitude of the Australian Scotch kirk con-
science towards gambling is a most humorous
contribution to the discussion on that subject.
Meenister Miller started out to tell the Meen-
isters’ Association that “ gambling is one of the
open sewers in the national life of the people,”
a vice with which Christians should have noth-
ing whatever to do in any shape or form. It
is thievery pure and simple, even when it
takes the form of a church-bazaar lottery or
lucky-bag. In the same breath, the learned
theologian declared that “ the spirit of gamb-
ling and speculation ” is, “ to a certain extent,
necessary for the prosecution of business, and
for the development of the resources of the
country.” Also, on the other hand, the Stock
Exchange brokers are “dangerously close to
distinct gambling-!” Minister Clouston has
a holy hatred of gambling, but “ can trace a
very decided difference between a raffle in a
church bazaar and a sweepstake on a horse-
race.” So can most people, inasmuch as the
sweepstake is generally worth winning, and the
church raffle isn’t. All of which is analytically
interesting as showing how tenderly accommo-
dating the kirk conscience is on deep matters of
duty. In short: “ Thy raffle is a Satanic abom-
ination and hateful to the Loard ; ma lottery is
a trusting enquiry consairning the will of (lord”
The international monetary conference is about
to sit somewhere down under, and the Agents-
General, by instruction, are clamoring to get
Australia a seat at the board. By whose
authority the governments are making this
move, nobody knows. What opinions the
Premiers want given utterance to, nobody
knows. What currency complexion the repre-
sentatives is to have, nobody knows. Is he to
be a monometallic worshipper of Supply and
Demand ? Is he to be a bimetallist who believes
Asia can be dished on the exchanges ? Is he to
be a gold man, a silver man, a paper man—or
a man of straw? Is he to vote with the best
dinners —or with the biggest coronets ? What
good will he do, anyhow, whoever he is ? The
Premiers have no warrant for making the
appointment, even if it is of no importance to
anybody, as is most probable. Some of the
Premiers, no doubt, regard it as a mere
advertising dodge. But it may be a
dodge to foist a choice assortment of
currency fads on Australia. It is very diffi-
cult to see why these provinces should get
mixed up in the matter at all. There has been
no popular expression of opinion on the subject,
and the only possible excuse for the offidal
action is that one or two of the Premiers think
it is a good opportunity to make a splash. They
are making the splash for all it is l.'trth, which
is not much.
The inhabitants of Eaglehawk (V.v.) and dis-
trict have achieved a rather startling vindica-
tion of the Law’s majesty. Eaglehawk raised
£l2 10s. for the purpose of presenting a
mounted policeman with a token of regard.
The promoters and trustees of this fund pur-
chased a gold locket and chain valued at £lO,
and “ detained ” the odd 50s. “in payment of
expenses incurred in soliciting subscriptions ”
whilst the jeweller who supplied the goods
made out his bill for £l2 10s., and gave a
receipt in full. It was a mean transaction.
The custodians of the cash had no authority to
spend 50s. in liquor, and, considering that they
put themselves forward as the policeman’s
warmest admirers, their action was dishonest
both in sentiment and fact, even though they
may not have made sixpence profit by it. Also,
the jeweller was wilfully aiding an attempt to
deceive the subscribers at the policeman’s ex-
pense. A charge of conspiracy to defraud
was laid against the two subscription-
gatherers and their friend the jeweller. They
were committed for trial, and the jury
some of whom doubtless took a purely Victorian
view of the case, and saw no guilt in a small
“ fakement ” of accounts —couldn’t agree upon
a verdict. Then the three prisoners in turn
pleaded guilty to avoid the mental strain of
another trial, and begged to be let off with
fines. Judge Hodges, with sorrow writ large
on his beautific countenance, proceeded to fine
the prisoners as suggested. The leading trustee
and prime mover in the 50s. conspiracy was
ordered to pay £3OO to the State, whilst the
other two men dropped in for a penalty of £SO
apiece. These sums were forthcoming and the
guilty trio went free to ponder over their folly-
in dealing unfairly with a trumpery subscrip- <
tion fund. “ Swindle ” seems a harsh term to
apply to their misdoing. It was not proved
that the trustees had deliberately robbed any-
body, or that the jeweller had been ready ito
assist in covering up a theft. Their joffit crime
w r as the want of a proper sense of honor
’in the outlay of other people’s money—the
crime which has been committed with impunity
in Victoria when the amount of money con-
cerned was a hundred thousand times greater
than £l2 10s. Nevertheless, it is refreshing
to hear of justice been done, or overdone, for
the satisfaction of any trusting Victorians,
whose confidence was, in a measure, betrayed.
The Crown, which prosecuted in this case, will
doubtless invite complaints from ruined bank
depositors who confided their gold to conscience-
less financiers. Their good “stuff” was in-
vested in property at false valuations. Unjust
stewards have devoured their substance. The
Crown may now see fit to bring the culprits to
book, and perhaps the learned Judge will sum
up unfavorably to the accused. The three
delinquents of Eaglehawk are cordially advised
by The Bulletin to start a society for the en-
couragement of justice all round.
Supply and demand, “red in tooth and
claw with r.iviiiH,’’ shrieks against the creed of
Socialism. It hungers for daily sacrifice of human
victims, and Fat Man is always ready to he high
priest.

My Dkar Moouabivda—
Everything: seems to conspire nowadays against
Sydney belledom’s chances of getting off the
tenter-hooks of spinsterdom on to the spikes of
matrimony, and the latest and greatest set-hack
seems to me to be the reduction in V. R. allow-
ances recently ukased by Government. The
salaries and allowances —the whole gilded bait, in
fact, attached to the position of A.’sD.C. and
private secretaries —are to be withdrawn, a step
which will save Government -81759 per annum,
but which will not, I venture to assert, prove
economical to the society house-father with a
quiverful of girls.
Looking at the matter from a girl’s point of
view, it would have been distinctly cleverer to
have directly reduced the Governor himself, who is
always married, and a non-starter for the
hymeneal plate,- and left the frequently-
eligible secretaries and aides unshorn. The
aides, above all, for they seem strangely addicted
rushing into matrimony with the white
aborigines—far more so than the secretaries, who
perhaps see too much of the mechanism in the
practice of their calling to ever feel deeply in love
with the daughters of women who grovel for paste-
board permits— not to speak of the little side-
struggles “on their own ” up the social ereasy-pole
on the part of the dear girls themselves, lint now
an inferior brand of Guvmentouse attache, on
something very like board-wages, threatens to
swamp the market, unless the very rosiest chance
happens and some pursey youth is attracted to the
job as a good way to get rid of time and money.
If the Ministry sticks to its resolution, future
aides will be either very rich men or very poor
ones—you may take my word for it.
But, with fuel and lighting allowances also with-
drawn, the new arrangement doesn’t promise an
extra-lively V.R. regime for the Potts Point
brigade. Society may have to strike wax-matches
to find its partner in the gubernatorial salons of
the near future, and it is just possible that winter
guests will be advised on their invitations to bring
Japanese pocket-warmers along with ’em. Also,
the announcement that the State pays for all the
wear and tear to Govt. House fixings will be a
blight on the vindictive variety of lady who has
been known to revenge herself fora dull vice-regal
entertainment and a lack of partners by kicking
the furniture with her sharp French heels. Now
every inch of varnish she peels off is damaged at
the expense of her bleeding country.

I thought the revival of “La Masootte,” at
Sydney Lyceum, suffered from being just a soup§on
too blue, though the first act was tolerably free
from the heavenly color. Nellie Stewart wore a
barber’s-pole petticoat, and what passes for rags
in Comic opera (a Masootte’s luck doesn’t seem to
do much for her own wardrobe, by the way) and
looked bewitching, as usual. Nellie S. is one of
those rare actresses who are never seen at a dis-
advantage. Broadside-on, upside-down, or any-
how, she is a vision of delight. Florence Young
cut a fine stage figure as Fiametta, in a green
velvet (purely comic opera) riding-habit with puff-
ball sleeves of pale green satin and a big cavalier
hat with white plumes ; and Mr. Brownlow,
radiantly attired in goatskin, reminded one of the
he-figure in “ Wedded,” oidy that the handsome
W. B. makes love down Nellie Stewart’s back.
By the way, the famous “ Glou Glou ” duet went
splendidly on the first night. Any young couple
with a limited knowledge of the art of canoodling
should make a point of inspecting the Glou Glou
patent before purchasing elsewhere. Though the
words may be only a translation from the French,
the “ business ” certainly isn’t.
Blueness arose in Act 2. The shapely pages
what lovely legs are May Pollard’s—were blue.
T.ipley was blue—his striped doublet gave him
the appearance of a boneless wonder from the
back ; Nellie S.’s white satin dress had a kind of
Masonic apron and collar of blue silk—blue feather
tufts and blue shoes and stockings ; and some of
L uiri Laurent’s jokes even were tinged with tur-
quoise. In that act, if you weren’t blue you had
to be yellow. Vernon and Florence Young re-
sembled a pair of cayenne-pepper-fed canaries,
and when Brownlow changes] his clothing he wore
a white suit slashed with pumpkin-yellow. It
really looked as if the co. hail individually lost
confidence suddenly in its own taste and started
to copy violently. In the last act Nellie
Stewart made a brief, all-too-fleeting appearance
in radish tights of the most long-drawn-out des-
cription (it is corsage revelations Miss Stewart
detests: ‘‘display as much leg as you like but
keep to high-water-mark above the brisket” is
Nellie’s idea of decency) as if to show that there is
no deception, ladies and gentlemen. The act is
played out in a white muslin bridal frock, while
Florence Young looks gorgeous in cactus colors.
Talking of stage dresses, I much regret that,
owing to being in such a wild whirl of drapery, I
put the wrong actress into a pretty frock during the
B. and B. season at .Sydney Lyceum. It was not
Miss Hammond who wore the ‘’cheerfully pretty
pink and blue dress” in the “ Ideal Husband;”
but another of the stage guests—a Miss Mina
Bouohier, an untamed amateur from one of the
Points, who owns the smiling garment in question
and writes to say so. Tuerefore, I have much
pleasure in publicly handing back the lady her
pink and blue vestment with the most contrite
apologies.

Dr. Emily Brainerd Ryder M.D.’s jack-o’-
lantern discourse (touching everywhere but guid-
ing you nowhere) “for women only,” in Sydney
Town Hill, on Monday afternoon, must have
keenly disappointed the mob of inquisitive-looking
women who turned up. Dr. Ryder is the direct
opposite of good old Dr. Anna Potts. Not only
because she sports a cap and gown, and rings on
both hands, while Anna P. wore semi evening
dress and a silky little blue feather fan, but be-
cause Dr. Ryder smilingly preaches the cheerful,
if somewhat debateable, doctrine that health is
largely a matter of will—while Dr. Potts, with her
mouth dog-eared at both corners, shook her head
dolefully at tlie eternal feminine and prophesied
unavoidable torments. Of the two, I think lugu-
brious Mrs. Potts would make a bigger success of
the women-only business. For one thing, that
mournful prophetess of evil had a cleverer knack
of introducing the unspeakably cerulean into her
lectures, and there was also a soothing “poor
dear ” air about her ghastly revelations that is
infinitely more welcome to most ladies than Dr.
Ryder’s breezy assertions that women needn’t be
yellow in irtyrs unless they choose. For my part,
I believe in neither of these itinerant medicine-
women. It strikes me as being the funniest thing
in the world to hear a woman “jawing ” off a plat-
form to several hundred pale and weary-looking
fe n des and inviting them, with an airy wave of
her be liamonded hands, tocomeon to the “health
plane”—just as if you achieved mens sam in
cor,/ore sano by taking the first turning to the
right.
Of course, every intelligent human being knows
that we can do a great deal towards our own
general good health by means of diet, careful
clothing, rest, and ail the other slow-coach things
that m ike the elixir of life merely an insipid dose
measured out in a medicine-glass—but for anyone
to assum ■ that we can largely prevent the spread
of disease simp’y by “taking tlie pledge not to
speak of it" is irritating optimism. Though I
supp )se Dr. Rv I t is quite right in preaching the
doctrine of cheerfulness, even if she is somewhat
inconsistent—first encouraging women to think
mure of them wives and less of their hushinds (a
statement which elicited a round of applause that
augured ill, in (thought, for the house-father) and
a moment afterwards congratulating NS.W.
women upon their rosy chance of getting a vote
“ when they will be even more helpful than be-
fore.”
If the accumulated duties of nursing, cooking,
sewing, washing and teaching, which (as Dr.
Rvder points out) are worrying the Australian
married women (it was comforting for spinsters to
hear that all Dr. R.’a warnings were hurled at the
married, by the way) into an early grave, surely
the latter end of women will he worse than the
first, if they ad 1 to these feminine deeds of
domestic daring a determination to poke their
noses into men’s m itters too? Even if women do
get the vote, tlie world will still have to be darned,
washed, fed and taught, and the women will con-
tinue to do most of these duties, although the
following advt. did appear in last Saturday's S.M.

The most depressing part of Dr. R.’s very
’Murkan discourse was when she solemnly advised
you to disbelieve that any disease is incurable
except old age; hut though every doctor had
fallen away exhausted from wrestling with your
case, all you had to do was to place your measles
or whatever it wgs in the hands of Providence and

When “ Paul Jones” succeeded “ An Ideal Hus-
band,” at Sydney Lyceum, one Sydney daily re-
marked, in that sublimely well-meaning way which
signifies nothing at all, that “the cleverness and
attract! veness of ‘An Ideal Husband ’ madeitmore
effective commercially than any other production
of the company.” A pretty theory, is it not?
But the real reason why folks Hooked to Oscar
Wilde’s spun-out p ay could scarcely be because of
its cleverness, while an exquisite piece like
“Rebellious Susan” was so poorly attended.
The fact 5 s that Boucicault carefully expunged
Wilde’s name from the bill, but not until everybody
knew who wrote the “ Ideal Hubby;” and Society,
scenting social carrion, rushed the playhouse to
gaze morbidly on a morally-shameless performance
in much the same spirit that the “ outside vulgar”
look at the cup and saucer of some noted criminal
and invest the innocent. d"lf with -A' r '
rible attributes suggested by their exoited imagin-
ations, As some people rush to get a twist of the
rope that hanged a murderer, so Society fell over
itself in its eagerness to discover if, in
the “Ideal Husband,” it might bo able to con-
vict the d irkly-accused dramatist out of his own
month. Because of underlying unsavoriness, the
“Husband ” was B. & B.’s strongest card—and
herein I think I discover a rattling good tip for
Unsuccessful authors.
Yours affectionately.
SAPPHO SMITH.

“La Mascotte " will have finished its
meteoric career at Sydney Lyceum, and the “ Gon-
doliers ” will have taken its place by the time
these few well-chosen remarks reach the public
eye ; and by Saturday the "Gondoliers ” will also
have been succeeded by the “ Mikado,” which will
go off with a sudden razzle-dazzle before The
Bulletin has time to express an enlightened
opinion concerning it. Under these circumstances,
the present observations had better be considered
in the light of ancient history. "La Mascotte”
opened on Saturday to a house that was crowded
in every part, thereby proving that, so long as
Nellie Stewart forms the centre, it doesn’t matter
much what kind of an opera is built around her.
Despite its many attractions, this ancient work
would fall flat with anyone save Our Nellie for its
leading character. The part of Bettina fits her to
perfection. She can make more music out of the
two little wooden shoes than anybody else, and no
one can exhibit the torn frock to more advantage,
or do the famous " Glou Glou ” duet quite so
prettily. Tnis paper’s private opinion is that the
duet in question is drivel, pure and simple, but
when perpetrated by Nellie Stewart it doesn’t
matter whether it is drivel or not. No one else
ever played Bettina with such joyous abandon,
and all through Miss Stewart formed the larger
half of the “Mascotte.” The principal part of
the other half was Lauri, as the miserable and car-
rotty monarch of Fiombino. Howard Vernon’s
Kooco was again hardly up to his old standard.
Brownlovv was in excellent voice, and his son! was
evidently warmed to enthusiasm by the frequent
privilege o‘ clasping Nellie Stewart to his copious
bosom ; but, though he made a dashing Pippo, yet
that agricultural lover is not of very much real
importance in theopera, Tapley, who hasn't been
doing anything very surprising of late, made a
great hit as Frittelini, Doth as regards his singing
and his funny little skip; and Miss Florence
Young bio lined forth into new beauty and p ayi d
Fiametta with remarkable success. Some of the
old puns were mercifully omitted, to the relief of
everybody concerned.

The same well-meaning Deputy-Ex. and a
still larger amount of suite is announced to attend
on Thursday at Her Majesty’s, where the magni-
ficent “ Faust ” is still running, and that spectacle
also dies on the succeeding night. This tendency
on the part of a vigorous and healthy production
to collapse immediately it has been seen by the
Acting-Governor is an eloquent commentary on
the cussed ness of monarchical institutions.

Rickards’new humorist, Horace Wheatley,
“engaged at an enormous salary,” was tried last
Saturday, and proved to answer well. Miss Ada
Delroy, the “greatest serpentine dancer in the
world,” as per Rickards’ advertisement, also
caught on, and if not the greatest serpent-lady on
earth she is at least sufficiently great for all
ordinary purposes. The champion bone soloist
made his first appearance on the same evening,
and people who liked bone soloists, and who
knew what a Hone soloist really ought to be,
seemed to approve of him cordially. For the rest,
Larry Duoley is still on hand, and Janies N A ie,
the tenor gentleman whose voice is so much bcuter
than his acting, appears next Saturday.

A new company, managed by Cosgrove
and Steuart, proposes to begin playing “Fun on
th•* Bristol” at Sydney School of Arts next
S itur lay. So far, there has been only one Widow
O’Brien in Australia, though several people have
endeavoured to be another, and have failed miser-
ably—the worst failure of the lot being six feet of
misery which attempted the character for one
consecutive night some years ago, supported by a
terrified looking girl in a drab frock. The next
worst failure was a haggard person who never
smiled, either then or afterwards. In consequence
of these and other failures the public as a whole
disbelieves in any Widow except the original
Sheridan. These remarks, however, are not in-
tended to discourage, but only to warn, the new
company.

“ Crom-a-boo,” the irresistibly tunny and
rollicking Irish song of flic Hoben Brothers, which
Sappho Smith opined would prove “a terrible
success,” is already proving. Lonnen has taken
it up in London, and Lanri is to sing it in
Australia; it is to be well introduced in America,
and in Maoriland. where that clever comedian,
Percy, of the Foliar! Lilliputians, is making a
feature of it. It is also a universal success and
heard everywhere. Paling’s have already cleared
the first big edition, and have got out two others
in E Hat and F. It is certainly the funniest Irish
football-song ever written, and the press every-
where has risen to describe it as the best comic
song of the day, and funnier than “Killaloe ” or
“ Ballyhooley.” It is just the thing tor an aspir-
ing coinique, pro. or amateur.*

** * *
“ A Gaiety Girl,” at Melb. Princess’s, still
bewitches a host of nightly admirers. At the
same time the public are by no means unanimous
as to the merits of the production. Naturally,
the phases of British fashionable life which
are so scathingly satirised are unfamiliar, on
the whole, to Australian audiences, and for
this the unobtrusiveness of the mounting
and the dressing fails to fully compensate.
To those who do “catch on,’’ however,
the “Gaiety Girl ” is wholly delightful. The
adorable Miss Maud Hobson divides honors as
first favorite with Miss Grace Palotta, the charm-
ing French maid. The worldly-wise Dr. Brierly
(Harry Monkhouse) also grows on public approval,
as well as that gay old dog, Major Barclay
(Fred. Kaye). Miss Madge Rossell’s dance in the
first act is a revelation, and always in short
supply. The musical burlesque, “In Town,’’ will
be put on for the first time on Saturday evening.
** * m
At Melb. Royal, the “ Span of Life ” con-
tinues to fulfil the promise of the first week.
Crowded houses assemble nightly to denounce the
villainies of Dunstan Leech, while little brother
Cecil eats his poisoned grapes to the accompani-
ment of sobs, hisses and much pathetic sniffing.
Walter Baker still bears himself gallantly through
the four acts, and, from the moment he rings the
lighthouse bell, inspires an undying (it hopeless)
passion in the bosom of many fair ones. Like-
wise, he fills many manly hearts with deadly hate
when he imprints burning kisses on the sweet lips
of Miss Hilda Spong. The nightly enthusiasm
culminates with the Living Bridge. Nobody
seems to tire of the Living Bridge, and quite a
number of Melbourne youths have sprained them-
selves badly while reproducing this sensation in
the domestic back-yard.
#* * *
The rather sensational exit of the Dacres,
at Melb. Bijou, Arthur Dacre coming before the
curtain to impeach theniianagement with numer-
ous sins of omission and commission (but
notably the former) was the most exciting event
of the nine weeks’ season. Rumor had prepared
the public for some unpleasant disclosures, and
they got even more disclosures than they expected.
Circumstances and internal dissensions and
muddlement have been against the Dacres ever
since they left England. “The Wife’s Ordeal”
seriously damped the enthusiasm which had been
worked up on Miss Roselle’s behalf, and thus the
season was practically wrecked almost as soon
as it started. The Dacres will probably be taken
up shortly by a Sydney manager. Meanwhile,
Melb. is working up a benefit as a tribute to the
sterling artistic merits of Miss Amy Roselle.

The serial acrobatics, the stronsr man Hij-k, and

The Wild-Cat Column
The remarks of J tidge Mol ••‘■■worth, of Melh.
Insolvency Court, as to the poliliciuly-dependeut condi-
tion of the Vic. minor Bench, should cause the com-
munily to-insist (Tub Bulletin lias constantly tinted this
for years past) that all Judges and Magistrates be placed
on the aime footing as the Supreme Court Bench. The
spectacle of a political head of a dept, which embraces
Judges and Magistrates amongst its officers, awakens
thought of what might occur if the head strained his
power. Recent events should induce the Legislature to
place the fount of justice beyond even suspicion of con-
tamination. The fact of the junior partner of a police-
court firm being Premier of the province opens one’s eyes
to the possibilities of the future. At present we see
the Premier acting as solicitor for a firm cast in heavy
damages for serious trade frauds ; the Attorney-General
acting as a counsel, having drawn the pleadings ; and,
iater on, these, io their political sphere, will be deciding
the question of mimical action against these people!!
F r rtunat -ly, Judge Molesworth, having a large private
fortune, is free from any pressure that might h placed
on one less wealthy, and it is to lie hoped that the
public will rake up the matter warmly. The corrup-
tion of the Vic. honorary justices’ bench is, and has been
for many years, notorious. Let the Victorian people lake
all precaution that the gangrene does not extend boa
higher quarter.

The National Bank of Australasia (like the
Colonial and the City of Melbourne) presents a balance-
sheet which is many degrees worse than its last one.
Apparently the past six months have been the worst time
the Melh. hanks have vet experienced. Tire National is
an institution of high repute and no suspicion is
entertained concerning its soundness, but, its latest
figures are not cheering. For the three preceding half-
years it made net profits of t'2s 300, £31,000, a d
£25,230 respectively, making the excellent total of nearly
£‘82,000 in 18 months of hard d pression. A'd of this
amount it deliberately fooled away nearly 1 72,000 in divi-
dends, mil to >k no'thought for the. morrow. The Bul-
letin denounced each one of these divs as an act of
f'lllv, especially as a large proportion of the reconstruc-
tion expenses were held over in order to pan t'i -m a ct if
sees no reason to alter its opinion. The net profits fo
the half-year Just ended are only £15,120—a falling-off of
£lO,OOO as compared with last half. Of this t7Bao goes
to pay the balance of reconstruction expenses, which
should have been extinguished before any division of
profits was made, and a 5 per cent, dividend on
preference shares absorbs all the rest and also
maxes it necessary to lake £3OO from profit and
loss account.' The ordinary shares, after receiving three
dividends on a steadily declining scale of 4J per cent., 4
percent, and 2J per cent., now recehe notiuiig at all.
Still the National, true to its former policy, “ blevvs ” all
the profits it has and takes no thought for the morrow. A
wiser directorate would have passed the dividend
altogether.
In every other respect the National looks ex-
ceedingly solid. Last ha f-ye ir s enormous cash balance of
£1,573,000 has increased to £1,655,000, which is altogether
too much cash to keep lying idle, though it represents a
lot of reserve strength. The item, “ Government deben-
tures,” has increased in the six months from £601,000 to
£70>,000, and if the management could see iis way to
invest another half-mdhmi in this direct on, and thereby
earn another £15,000 or more per annum tor the share-
holders, or else pay off half a mdlion of fixed deposits, it
would do a good turn to the institution. The ordinary
business is evidently improving, the bank having got no
less than £2,111,000 of deposits since its suspension. And,
in addition, it has a reserve-fund of £lOO,OOO. It the
board will only mitigate its hunger after dividends and
huge cash balances, all promises to be well.
♦ • • *
The Colonial Bank of Australia follows the ex-
ample set by the City of Melbourne, and intends aban-
doning the hopeless struggle to pay 4£ per cent, interest
to its reconstructed depositors. An appeal is therefore to
be made for a reduction—probably to 3 per cent. The
reasons for this painful proceeding are sufficiently evi-
denced by the balance-sheet, which shows the same pro-
cess of fading away which has distinguished the other
ill-fated reconstruct aDove-mentioued. for the half-year
ended March, ’94, the Colonial made a net profit of £7353.
The half ended September, ’94, showed a net profit of only
£2246. Last six months shows a net loss of £4694—and
this despite a great reduction in working expenses and a
considerable increase of capital. The gross profits have
dropp-d from £40,000 a year ago to £lB,OOO. The Colonial
has written off its reserve-fund of £309,000, and, when the
Companies Act has been refixed, it proposes to write off a
portion of its capital—how much doesn’t 3 7 et appear. In
the first year after reconstruction it fooled away £ll,OOO in
paying two ghostly little diva. on preference shares, which
proceeding Tub Bulletin declared to be unjustifiable and
ridiculous. As the event proves, it was worse than ridicu-
lous. The div. wasn't earned in any legitimate sense : it
was simply arrived at by,-omitting to provide for
impending losses. The big dailies, however, professed
to regard these dividends as a sign of returning prosperity.
The Colonial has .£12,000 less note-circulation
than it had a year ago ; £32,000 less bills in circulation ;
£220,000 less deposits ; £130,000 less cash ; and £4OOO
less Government debentures—all signs of a steady shrink-
age in business. It is a good deal further away from any
prospect of paying iff its reconstructed deposits when
they fall due than it seemed to be 18 months ago. The
paid-up ordinary capital, too, is only £500,000, which
leaves no very large margin for writing otf, and the
exceeding feebleness of its shares and deposits on the
market shows that the public views it with a great lack
of enthusiasm. Still, if the deposit interest cache reduced
to (say) 3 per cent., the saving will be something between
£25,000 and £30,000 per annum, and with such a load
lifted off its shoulders even a very weak hank ought to
have a fair chance of recovery, always provided the de-
cline in business doesn't go on b definitely. How the
depositors feel on the subject.is,a different matter. These
luckless individuals already lose £13,500 per annum by
the conversion of part of their interest-hearing deposits
into preference shares, which pay no div., and are not
likely to pay any f)r long to come. If the interest on the
balance is reduced to 3 per cent., their total loss will be
some £4O 000 a year. It is a lovely thing to be a recon-
structed depositor.
