The City of Melbourne Bankvdirectors reports
joyously that “ the liquidation of the old hank has been
completed.” The same directors report that owing to-
the stagnation of the real estate market they can’t tell
the value of many of the old bank’s assets, and that they
are waiting for an amendment of the Companies Act to
enable them to write off a lot of capital to cover the old
bank’s losses—amount of losses not yet fully ascertained.
Under these circumstances bow is the liquidation com-
pleted ? Apparently the old bank lias simply been
shovelled bodily into the new one without any liquidation
at all.
• » * *
According to the latest returns, the Queensland
Government has now a small trifle of £4,300,000 in the
Q.N. Bank, less than half of it being “fixed” and the
rest (about £2,220,000) nominally free. Also, it has
£215,000 in other banks. Yet the Govt, professes itself
unable to pay off £1.100,000 of treasury bills falling due
next year without floating a new loan ! The Q.N. Bank,
by the way, only professes to hold £1,500,000 in coin and
notes, including its assets in London, so perhaps the
Govt, isn’t such an ass as it looks, after all.

The Abrahams have yet to meet the charge of
criminally infringing trade-marks, tbe hearing of which
was postponed until after tbe trial of Greener’s case
against them. The Greener civil action will probably
coat the defendants between £BOOO and £9OOO. The
damages awarded were £6500.
The advance in wheat means the salvation of
many a Vic. country storekeeper. The majority of them,
despairing of receiving payment in hard cash from their
custom rs, were forced to take grin as contra. So
desperate were affairs in many districts that live stock,
any kind of produce, in fact anything of value, actually
took the place of currency. It was nothing but the
old days over again,

Truth in mining, when unsatisfactory, is very
slow, hut it has com out at lasi in re Mr. Drysdale mine.
J. B. Jacqu-t, N.S.W. geologist-surveyor, reports that
the rich ore in Mt. Drysdale thinned out and terminated
at 160 ft., and the last. 100 tons sent to Queensland only
yielded lOdwts. per ton. The ore occurs in three shoots,
separated by m isses of barren rock. He thinks it p >ssihle
that the ore-body may lie picked up lower down but below
tile zone of oxidation ; it will probably contain less gold
and be more difficult to treat. Yet for months after the
fact was established Ihe public were told that the mine
was “as rich as ever.” The press is the medium of the
biggest mining crams, and the same old game recurs with
everv new gold-mine or field.

probate; nor can he in equity claim, or obtain,
much more than yis own valuation. A 1 the alluvial
is found in gullies, which in the course of natural
events—to wit, yearly rainfalls —will fill up. The
balance of holes I wilt take a contract to-morrow to
fill up for £5O, so that should the field to-morrow prove
an utter, Fingall failure, the Government stands at the
worst to lose but a few pounds It is a poor man’s field ;
nothing startling has been, or is It;My to be found, but
i k has kept 500 stomachs filled fer 10 months, and looks
likely to do so for 24 mo tbs mo e, after which the work
Ini I ctnen on the reefs till now discover d will occupy
some 200 men for another three yeais at l°ast. R sump-

who will take up resid nee areas, and s ttle flown,
therebv giving revenue in many ways ; also a s'imnlns to

men thrown on the overstocked labor-market, more ex-
penses in free f ires, rations and miners’ lights, and general

On visiting a mine in southern N.S.W., the
other day, the expert saw at once that it was chiefly a
Hebrew concern. The machinery consisted of a Jordan
pulveriser and amalgamator, the pulveriser being a pan
with three halls.

Want of wat°r is stopping crushing* and bring-
ing down the price of shares throughout Wyalong.
Miners’-right ar as are perhaps big enough for legitimate
mining but bad for booming. London loves a lot, and as

speculation Wyalong can only have a boom by amalgama-
tion of areas or by permitting leases.

Queen’s Birthday mine, Dnnolly (Yin.), which
lately recommenced with such a flourish of trumpets,
must be in a had way. A large number of men were
engaged and, after working a couple of months, found
that the English capital to pay them was not forth-
coming; so work has again been stopped, • with about
£5OO owing to the mmeis. The whole of the plant,
leases, &e., is mortgaged to British debenture-holders for
£10,500, and these will have to pav the men unless the
shareholders ante-up, as wages are a first charge in
mining, even hef re mortgage.

The splendid success of Harris’Reward mine at
Mount .bikes, Tas., has stimulated the flotation of the Mt.
Jukes Proprietary to work adjoining and 'djacent ground.
Thi« co. has secured no less than 140 acres of ground, in-
cluding several blocks directly on the line of Harris’ reef,
which carries gold into one‘of them. If the gold goes
down, and there is plenty of stone, such an acreage should
pay good divs. on the £15,000 capital.
In whatever direction the bias of the
Sydney police may lie with respect to the Dean
case, it is plain, from confidential information
which has reached The Bulletin from a well-in-
formed Victorian quarter, that tho Melbourne
detectives are by no means in love with the Sydney
Crown Law Office theories. It would appear that
in the Dean case history is likely to repeat itself,
somewhat on the lines of the startling Biittner
case. The condamne in the latter case, by the
way. owed his life to the Brisbane detectives’
enquiries.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION,
It has long been known to scientists' that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists lias been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby's Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may he had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

Mr. T. J. Santety, Murrumburrah, who writes on
June 13th, 1893 About four months ago I was
greatly troubled with a continued languid feeling,
also loss of appetite, costiveness ; in fact, I felt
myself hardly capable of carrying on my usual
business, owing to a tired feeling ; I awoke in the
morning more unrefreshed than when I went to
bed. A friend of mine, who was boarding in the
same house as me, who had been benefited by
taking Clements Tonic for the above symptoms,
advised me to try it. I did so, and am happy to
say that before I had finished the third bottle all
disagreeable symptoms had disappeared. I am
happy to say I have never had any occasion to
take any medicine since. I consider it is one of
the greatest panaceas in the world to those suffer-
ing from indigestion or affection of the liver.
Thanking you for the great benefit I have derived
from the use of your wonderful Clements Tonic.
I remain, yours, &c., T. J. Santety, tailor, Albury-
street, Murrumburrah.

time—among them Nelson, Reid and Turner —
suffer from insomnia.

Carlyle in his Fronde, but Higinbotham more
than either in his Morris.

now exiled racehorse has, it is said, become en-
gaged to Melbourne’s richest widow.
A Sydney printer is setting up a volume
of irregular verse by Willoughby Andrews, the
crank assassin who figured before the public a few
years ago.

the office-boy of a business man dismissed for the
heinous crime of addressing the bebaubled one as
plain “ Mister.”

mentions that W. Nicholson, father of the ballot
in Victoria, began public life as a violent opponent
of that measure.

the other day, and the late Gov. Duff were the
only antipodean Governors who openly advocated

Premier Kingston ceased to be a sergeant
in the S. A. local military forces at the end of
April. He had been on leave of absence from his
soldierly duties for two years.
The Earl of Yarmouth is in Bananaland,
doing his level best to get up another charity
entertainment whereat he may disclose his pipe-
stem limbs in the serpentine-dance.
A popular Melbourne medico has just
married his mother-in-law. Real life imitates
fiction. As witness this occurrence after Jerome
Darnel’s Bulletin story, “The Bold M'Shane.”

ston, Gordon, and Gockburn, three of his col-
leagues, born in 1850, and so will lie 45 this year—
on May 12. Chief Justice Madden, ot Victoria,
will be 51 on May 16.
A daughter of the late Bernhard-Smith,
P.M. is a dressmaker in Melb. Smith, a sculptor
of. celebrity, was one of the pre-Raphaelite
brotherhood, and brought out the Germ (the
organ of the order) with Dante Rossetti and Hol-
man Hunt.
A Melb. pressman strolled into the
Melb. Metropolitan Board of Works
office on business, and, seeing only a clerk
scribbling at a side table, inquired ; “ Is
the chairman knocking about?” Great
Panjandrum himself at once appeared
and announced, in withering tones :
“ The chairman is about, sir, but he is not
knocking !”
Melbourne Judges have decided
that Duffy and Purves are getting really
too dreadful,, and must be muzzled when
in opposition in Court if they do nob
cease trying to he painfully smart with
each other at the expense of their clients
and the country at large. These two
luminaries never meet in a case now with-
out suffering violent outbreaks of humor ;
and the vast power of tue machinery of
the law runs to waste, and salaries of
the Judges, associates, policemen, and
supernumeraries accumulate, and the
costs swell visibly, and the wear and tear
of justice goes on, whilst Purves and
Duffy call e; ch other names, and per-
spire in their wigs, thinking out bright
bits of repartee to fling in each other’s
teeth. We all know the time occupied
in the hearing of Lange versus Bage,
and what the case cost; ditto in Speight
v. Syme; but an unemployed accountant
would do his country a signal service
by totting up the costs in time and cash
incurred in the hearing of Purves versus
Duffy and Duffy versus Purves. It would
be advisable for the future, in The Bul-
letin’s opinion, to refuse these leaders of
the Bar admission into the Courts until
they have fought fifteen hard rounds in
the back-yard, so that they may expedite
business in their anxiety to hurry home
and apply arnica to their hurt places.

In Brighton (Vic.) cemetery, last week, a
Bulletin correspondent saw half-a-dozen people
(visiting Maorilanders) at Lindsey Gordon’s grave.
One <>f them was standing on the grave reciting
“The Sick Stockrider ” to the other five.
Madge Rossell, of “ The Gaiety Girl” Co.,
appears to be the only lady member of that
beauteous throng who is not about to be married
presently. Yet the fortune-hunters should be
after Madge, for there is lots of money in her kick.
Thomas Elder, the S. A. _ millionaire bache-
lor, is seriously ill of a brain-trouble. Another
S. A. millionaire, W. H. Gray, is also very ill, and
awhile ago was not expected to live many weeks.
Elder’s fortune is approximated at over a
and Gray is credited with a nest-egg of .£1,500,000.
Fred. Want, who has just died ab Mon-
treux, did not long survive his brother, Ranny,
(writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent by
last mail). He had lately suffered from epileptio
trouble, and his lungs were much affected, but to
the last he showed the characteristic courage of
the family.
Colin M‘Kay Smith, the anti-Payrnent of
Members’ stalking-horse, ran a little newspaper a
couple of years ago from the premises of a big
Sydney printer. Mr, Smith is a really able man,
with a London Times manner of thought and
style of composition, and is said to have once been
a squatter.
In a recent appeal case, before Yic. Jus-
tices Williams, Holroyd, and Hood, a writ was
put in evidence. Justice Holroyd read it through,
and remarked that it was badly drawn and did
not disclose a good cause of action. Counsel
smiled, for the writ had been drawn by Judge
Hood when at the Bar.
Dear Bulletin,- -The Queensberry “ ec-
centricity ” dates as far back as three centuries
ago, when a mad scion of the house escaped, one
Sunday, from the domestic confinement in which
he was kept—killed the turnspit-boy—and was
dining on the carcase when “ the family returned
from church.”— Reekie.
As Rupert Carington proposes substan-
tially settling in N.S. Wales, “having very large
interests there which require his personal super-
vision,” it has been suggested that the N.S.W.
Government should, as a handsome and graceful
attention, include him in the first batch of
nominees to the Legislative Council !
Theo. Napier, the barbarian who habi-
tually paraded Melb. streets in kilts, showing the
awfulness of his scanty legs, is now in Scotland,
sowing sedition among the parritch-eaters. Ap-
pealing for funds for the Scottish Home Rule
Association, he styles himself “President of the
Scottish National Association of Victoria.”
Edward Chippendale, who came to N.S.
Wales with his parents in Gov. Macquarie’s time
(1813), has died ac Maitland, aged 83. Macquarie
gave deceased’s father a grant of what is now the
populous Sydney suburb of Chippendale. The
Chippendale just dead was a good judge of cattle,
and opened up a large horse-business with India.
Robert Stout, of Maoriland, declined to
appear as advocate for the Midland Railway Co.
in its law-suit against the M.L. Govt., although
offered 1000 guineas to do so. On the other hand,
he consented to appear as senior counsel for the
Govt, on the distinct understanding that he shall
receive nothing at all in fees or rewards for his
services.
Dear Bulletin, —Whatever may have
been the Australian origin or introduction of the
expression “ wealthy lower orders,” Marcus Clarke
can hardly have anything to do with it. Higin-
botham used it in a speech at Brighton in ’64,
when Marcus, an unknown boy of 17, buried in
the Wimrnera, had hardly been a year in Victoria.
—Yours, P.M.
At one of the Queensland stations visited
during Premier Nelson’s tour in the North-West,
the Premier was entertained in the house, while
his private secretary and the Courier special war"
sent to an out-building. To his credit be it said

that Nelson was nob slow to intimate that he did
not wish any better treatment than his fellow
travellers were accorded.
Ex-Reverend Tate, a South Sea Island
missionary, built the house which the Passionist
fathers now occupy as a monastery near Goulburn
(N.S.W.). Thespeculative ex-missioner committed
suicide in one of the rooms of the establishment,
a circumstance which depreciated the value of
the property to a figure that placed it within
reach of the Passionist.
It was Oapt. Mackay who first discovered
and opened up the rich sugar-lands bearing his
name; for this valuable service he has received
no reward whatever. In Oct., '92, 1000 acres of
Mackay sugar-lands were voted him by Q. Parlia-
ment, nem, dis. But the MTlwraith Govt, losing
power, the old navigator did not receive the pro-
mised reward, and probably he never will.
Mrs SEYMOUR (Mother-in-law of Dean,

A London correspondent ; —Tiger Inglis
has been seriously ill here from “suppressed
influenza,” but succeeded in launching his
Cremorne Coal Co. ere he was stricken down. A
versatile fellow is the Tiger. A couple of weeks
ago he presided at a dinner of the Article Club (a
kind of offshoot of Commerce), and made two of
the most amusing speeches I have ever listened to.
The N.S. W. Parliamentary question about
swearing, aimed at the wicked and downright
General Hutton, looks like a shaft from a feminine
bow. In certain society circles, the General is
hated with a hate unspeakable. He is always at
work and keeps other people at work, which
doesn’t suit the whisky-and-soda brigade—the
people with hob-nail livers and parchment kid-
neys who, ere the General’s advent, did nothing
but draw their fatted sa'aries.
In Basil Thomson’s book on Tonga, Rev.
Shirley Baker is thus immortalised: “Three
natives were indicted for stealing a pig. Two had
agreed to watch while the third stole.” “This,”
said the autocratic Baker, in his judicial function,
“ was no ordinary theft—it was conspiracy.” He
found his law-book defined conspiracy as a
synonym for treason, and the punishment for
treason was up to 20 years. Accordingly, the
prisoners were found guilty of treason.
Ex-Premier Parkes, says Sydney Sunday
Times, is collecting his letters for publication,
having kept a copy of almost every letter he ever
wrote. The Bulletin hopes to see in the collec-
tion the letters written by H. Parkes to the liar
who invented the Kiatna Ghost, and who, having
had large expenses paid for him by an enquir-
ing and patriotic government, at last refused to
give any more information, on the ground that the
Parkes Government didn’t address him as So-and-
So, “Esquire.”
Melbourne Argus remarks that “Phil
May developed his talent in Australia, although
he was not born here,” but the conscienceless rag
doesn’t mention that May’s ta'ent was monopolised
by The Bulletin during the development a'ore-
said. This is petty. In the'same paragraph the
Argus Writer refers to Perey Spence as an artist,
whose work,apart from the frontispiece hedesigne i
for the Antipodean, is not familiar to Victorians.
This is a cold-blooded lie. Spence’s drawings for
The Bulletin have been seen and admired by
thousands of Victorians for years past. For every
man who remembers the Antipodean frontispiece
there are a thousand who know the artist by his
contributions to this journal.
Many years ago the writer heard the late
Charles Badham, then Professor of Classics in
Sydney University, deliver an address. The
occasion has slipped his over-burdened memory,
but three sentences of the address have never
ceased to tingle through his brain. “If any
young man in this assembly,” said Prof. Badham,
“ has embarked his life in pursuit of knowledge,
let him go on without doubting or fearing the
event. Let him not be intimidated by the cheer-
less beginnings of knowledge, by the dark-
ness from which she springs, by the diffi-
culties which have surrounded her, by those
wretched habitations in which she dwells, by the
want and sorrow which sometimes journey in her
train; but let him ever follow her as the angel
that guards him, and as the genius of his life.
She will bring him out at last into the light of
day, and exhibit him to the world comprehensive in
acquirements, fertile in resources, rich in imagina-
tion, strong in reasoning, prudent and peaceful
above his fellows in all the relations and in all the
offices of life.” The speaker of those eloquent
words has long lain in his grave, but his spirit is
still a source of inspiration, and his family hand
down not unworthily the traditions of his lofty
talent. Mr. Charles Badham, his son, is P.M. at
Carcoar, N.S.W. Miss Edith Anesley Badham
(whose portrait appears in this week’s Bulletin)
is a clever University coach, and the author of
the very able and remarkable paper on Woman’s
Suffrage read at the last meeting of N.S. Wales
Economic Association. Another son, Dr. Bad-
ham, is studying bacteriology at the Pasteur In-
stitute, but will probably return to Australia this
year. Miss Julia Badham (the youngest of the
family) is a trained nurse, residing at Mosman’s
Bay, near Sydney, with her sister and her mother,
Mrs. Chas. Badham.
Recently at Maryborough (Vic.) the daugh-
ter of ex-Mines-Minister Outtrim was married,
and the cacchinatory Chief Secretary Peacock,
always young-looking, w ho was among the guests,
looked more youthful still when attired in full fig.
When he took up the pen to sign as a witness, the
clergyman called him aside and (not knowing him)
said, “ Pardon me, but it is usual for witnesses at
weddings to he over 21. Have you reached that
age?” To use Peacock’s own words —“I let one
laugh out that nearly lifted him off his feet.”
Messengers frequently cm me to Vic. Premier
Turner while in Court on the Greener case. Some
were from the Treasury and some were not. The
other day a fussy little man forced his way
through the crowd and laid a paper before the
Premier. It might have been a declaration of
war from S. Austra.ia over the disputed boundary,
or anything big and national. But it only con-
cerned a small insolvent-boomer’s estate, and it
was all the same to Turner. He wrote his opinion
across the back, and would have done the same
during a want-of-confidence debate.

Meston has written another little book
about the poor blacks, and dedicated it under the
stimulus of fellow-feeling to ‘‘the Honorable
Horace Tozer, Colonial Secretary of Queensland ’
—George Washington being unavailable. 'The
little book collects a lot of interesting information,
and comes to a climax at p. 30, win re it is pointed
out that a Protector of Aborigines should be at
once appointed by Govt. According to Meston,
it is “absolutely essential” that the Protector
“shall possess (1) a know], dge of aboriginal
character and customs (2) “ a naturally kind
disposition and great firmness and decision of
character;” (3) “a strong physique.” All other
varieties of Protector are bogus. And these con-
Photo, by Newman,
SIGNOR LARDELLI.
ditions unluckily vitiate the whole scheme, for
The Bulletin knows nobody but Meston himself
who complies with them ; and, though he fills the
bill, he would, of course, decline to fill the billet.
So the blacks must apparently continue to die off
and jump up whitefellow in the good old pre-
Mestonian way.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The sugar-industry progresses in Queens-
land. Govt, is calling tenders for a new lazarette.
Brisbane Courier has been advised to
place its leper-paragraphs under the beading of
“Our Industries.”
A parson writes to the Brisbane papers
advocating a close season for Kanakas. It should
be made to apply to the missionaries also.
Memo, for clever, but unlearned, R. E.
O’Connor: There was neither gallows nor “cat”
in Ireland while that country was ruled by Celts.
A delicate question now frequently asked
on the Freetrade side of N.S.W. Assembly is:
How will Adrian Knox vote on the sugar branch
of the tariff reform ?
“I am growing old and grey-haired look-
ing for work for others, and now I’m beginning
to think it’s time to look for some for myself.”
“ Billy ” Maloney, M.P.
Gov. Robinson’s suggestion that the
Westralian grant to the aborigines should be
placed on the same footing as the Governor’s
salary is an undeserved insult to the aborigines.
A gloss on N.S.W. Civil Service Commis-
sion’s report re the inefficiency of State audit is
supplied by the officer who is asserted to have just
admitted an account shortage of approximately
32250.
Should Premier Turner’s health break
down within the next month or two, it is believed
that either Isaacs, Best, or Deakin would succeed
him. Peacock, however, is regarded as a rapidly
“ coming ’’ man.
Tozer and Byrnes are the two rival
Ministers for the next Q. Premiership. Byrnes
wants the position ; Tozer wants either the posi-
tion or the knighthood. The chances are that
neither will get either.
Four successive cablegram headings in last
Saturday’s Sydney Telegraph : “ The Queensland
Loan,” “ A Reported Victorian Loan,” “ A West
Australian Loan,” and “ A Now Zealand Loan.”
Truly this is the Great Loan Land.
The feeling against H. H. Champion at
Melb. Trades Hall is confined to a few members,
led by Hancock and Prendergast. Champion
says he intends to fix himself in Victoria, and to
stand for Parliament at next election.
The trustees of Collins-street Independent
Church, Melb., are being sued for obstructing
neighbors’ air and light with certain church struc-
tures. If the act of obstructing air and light is
held to be illegal, what church is safe?
The N.S.W. military authorities are ac-
tively working up the .Japanese scare. The
beer-barrels used as artillery-targets at South
Head the other day were made to represent “ the
Japanese warships Naniwa and Yoshina.”
Montgomery and Williams struck fran-
tically for escape,and were hanged for “attempted
murder;” whereas the man who, the other day,
deliberately walked up to another and fractured
his skull with a bottle, is charged only with “in-
flicting grievous bodily harm.” But the botth -
user hadn’t been meddling with holy “ property ”
wliich makes all the difference !
Varney Parkes’ pamphlet on the Public
Works Committee, and his father’s on G.
H. Reid, so far afford no encouragement in
the financial sense to men of letters to enter
the field of political pamphleteering. “ The
Reid fraud” seems to pay better than the
write fraud.
There are two classes of politicians—
the'burly and the burley. Parkes and Bibbs
are.baokboneful and burly : Reid is spine-
less and burley. Burley is the stuff
you throw to black-bream, to make
them come round and bite. Reid is
always burleying the political black-
bream ground.

partment finds that the head-light on
the engine, which used to cost <£l
6s. 4d. per annum for oil, &c., can
he maintained for 3s. Id. ; saving,
<£2ooo yearly. Also <£l3oo a year

Two months ago N.S. W. Colonial Secre-
tary Brunker said that he had a list of 100 new
J.’sP. in hand. They are still in hand. “Grati-
tude is a lively expectation of favors to come.” A
J.P. in hand is worth two in the N.S.W. back-
blocks.
The Argus on Morris’s dainty handling of
George Higinbotham’s democracy:—“Professor
Morris has too much good taste to revive more
than is necessary forgotten fends.” Tastes differ,
and a taste of the real Higinbotham is gall to the
Argus,
The new N.S.W. Crown Lands Act has
passed both Houses of Parliament. Now the
dummies, the jobbers, the swindlers, the land-
thieves, the Fat Men, may set to work to find its
holes, and drive through with the same old
bullock-waggons,
Vic. Premier Turner has ordered that
future Civil Service appointments shall be on
monthly agreements, that there shall be no per-
manency, express or implied, and consequently
no compensation for loss of employment, and no
lairn for pension.
What does Ren wick’s expensive report of
the Chicago Exhibition mean? Listen to W. B.
Dailey on an analogous occasion : “It means a
most shameful extravagance of public money in
printing the memorials of Jones’s audacious ignor-
ance and intolerable self-sufficiency.”
Sydney D.T. : “There has never been a
case in which payment of members once sanc-
tioned has ever been abolished.” What about
England? Again: “Parliament may cut down
people’s salaries, but its own are always held
sacred.” What about Victoria and Queensland ?
The S.M. Herald objects to one-half, or
less than one-half, of the Assembly, at a late hour
of the night, when the Labor element prepon-
derates, passing Bills ; but it never objects to
one-half of the Council shelving or repealing Bills,
and less than one-half is . the normal constitution
of that body.
Spasm from Melb. Herald :
See and be proud of those men in the procession. Stal-
wart, well-dressed, happy-looking, erect, filled with the
glorious sense of manliness which is one of the happiest
products of this favored land.
As a matter of fact, the last was the most solemn
Eight-Hours procession Melbourne has seen. The
grim representation of a sweater’s den lent color
to the whole show, and never a cheer was heard
along the line of march. Looking on, one felt
that the funerals of the six men fished from the
Spottiswoode sewer would not have been out of
place at the heels of the army of labor. “The
glorious sense of manliness ” does not come out
strong on “ four bob a day,”
The Boodlewraifch has just ordered a
trial survey of the Hnghenden-Winton railway.
This will be a faial blow to Rockhampton supre-
macy. Most of tlie trade of the Hughenden-
Winton district at present drifts down to the
capital of Oentralia, and the proposed railway will
infallibly divert it to Townsville. Such is the
reward of fidelity to the hated Southern Govern-
ment.
The first arbitration carried out by the
State Board of Conciliation in Adelaide ended
ineffectually. The tanners’ dispute was en-
quired into and a unanimous finding by the Presi-
dent and the representatives of employers and
workmen was arrived at, the process being a
judicious distribution of blame among both parties
to the trouble. No tangible benefit resulted to
anyone.
Some of the Queensland papers anticipate
a big difficulty through Japanese immigration,
and say that Japan will not submit to anything
in the way of a poll-tax on her people. One paper
describes the Australian militia as “poorly-trained,
ill-disciplined, and armed with obsolete weapons,”
and reckons that Japan could —without British
intervention —run right over Australia if a war
took place.

In connection with N.S.W. Education
Minister Garrard’s school-age relapse from six
years to five, it is worth noting that of the 4J
United States and Territories six admit children
to the public schools at four years minimum, 1J
at five, 20 at six, three at seven, and one at eight.

males, 578,500 females ; total, 1,251.450. Sydney
and suburbs: 214,110 males, 209,490 females; total,
423,600 —or oue-third of the population of the pro-

ing handicaps on country producers. When bor-
rowing stops, if ever, on whom will the apex-
balanoed pyramid fall?

INSOLVENCY ITEM.
“Insolvent stated that she was a widow, and her estate was compulsorily
sequestrated on 8th Now. last. Her husband died in 86. They did not
divide the profits at all. They kept no hooks except to show the sales and
purchases. She had very little to do with the business. They only drew
out of the business house expenses and the hoys’ pocket-money. Each of
her sons took whatever money he liked, and so did she for maintenance
purposes, but no record was kept of the drawing of any of them, There
were no means of finding out even approximately the amount of those draw-
ings—not even an idea roughly. Whichever of them wanted to draw
money just went to the till and took whatever was wanted.”—Melb[?]ne
law-report.
Unless the N.S.W. Ministry walks circum-
spectly, it will find itself out of office over the
Dean case. What may smash it is not any want
of p'lability on the part of Premier Reid, for he
is a jelly-fish politician, into whom you can stick
a knife without either drawing blood or making a
subsequeutly-visible hole. The trouble may an-e
out of the fact that Attorney-General Want has
a backbone,” and will have no truck with popu-
lar clamour.”
In the Western district (Vic.) the muni-
cipalities which control local taxation are all
governed hy squatters. In Hampden shire seven
out of the nine councillors are squatters. The
Heyteshury forest is unrepresented. The pick of
the country held by the squatters is assessed at
3d. an acre ; the dense forest, where the selector
has been dumped, at 2s. 6d. an acre.

The Higinbotham-edited Argus utterance
on capital punishment is thus stated in Prof
Morris’s biography :
We are convinced that the abolition of capital punish-
ment should at least be tried, were it only for a time. It
would be easy to revert to barbarism should amelioration
appear to be premature. —December 10, 1858,
“This view,” Mr. Morris asserts, “ later j experi'
ence caused Mr. Higinbotham to change.” Only
that, and, in the following 250 pages, nothing
more 1 But how could anything change such an
unimpeachable and carefully-expressed philoso-
phical suggestion?

The Bulletin desires to caution people
Against publicly giving cheers for Dean, convicted
of poisoning, but generally believed to be guilt-
less. It would be much safer, as a vent for enthu-
siasm, to give groans for the system of hurried
trial. Cocksureti'-ss with regard to Dean’s inno-
cence might -suddenly result as awkwardly for
Dean and his partisans as revelations in an oppo-
site direction for the Judge who so loudly declared
his certainty of the man’s guilt. Dean may be
innocent or he may be guilty ; and, so far, there is
more reason for believing him innocent than
guilty. But the only things absolutely certain in
regard to Dean are that he had not a fair trial,
and that the evidence on which he was convicted
is not, looked at dispassionately, good enough to
hang a mangy dog.
By the way, why doesn’t Judge Windeyer
arrange another M lion seance, and ask “ Geordie”
or “ Cissy ” whether Dean really did it or not.
The spooks must know all about it. Perhaps it
was a spook dropped the arsenic in.
The support accorded (on this and other
memorable occasions) to Mr. Justice Windeyer’s
peculiar methods by his friend, the S.M. Herald,
in no wise balances the severe and almost un-
measured condemnation of the Argus and the
general press of Australasia. Nor do the anony-
mous letters, composed chiefly of typically
Heraldic suppressions and perversions of the
truth, with a strong admixture of downright im-
pudent lies, tend to bolster up the case on behalf
of His Honor. The protracted and wide-spread
discussion over this matter must be peculiarly
agonising to several people. In a case where a
man is hanged by a “backbone ” Ministry in de-
fiance of public opinion, and, it may be (it has
often been) of every principle of justice, the public
tumult usually ceases with the pulling of the bolt.
But the unhanged George Dean and his long-
drawn-out case seem to have been specially devised
for the purpose of destroying the good old Botany
Bay method of conducting capital trials.
Everywhere in this world amour propre is a
strong factor. How terribly awkward it would be
for the Judge, after his gratuitous declaration
that he was as sure Dean poisoned his wife as if
he had seen him in the act, were it to be con-
clusively shown that Dean’s was not the hand
which did the deed ! To err is human, but judges
of the Supreme Court, in their official capacity at
least, are not supposed to be as human as other
people. How his Honor’s nerves would be on the
rack if he knew that there was an absolute cer-
tainty of the North Shore mystery, for mystery it
is, being made clear (not clairvoyantly clear, or
Mellunically clear) in the sight of all men!
The Bulletin dreamt some nights ago that it
was the head of the detective-force, and met His
Honor in the street after it had absolutely solved
the problem. “Your Honor,” said this paper,
preserving a sphinx-like face, “it is now known
for certain where the poison came from and who
administered it.” His Honor tried hard to control
his emotions, but his eyes almost started out of
his head with excitement. “ What about it?” he
snapped. “Ah, your Honor,” replied theemotion-
less head of the detective-force, “ that is my busi-
ness for the present.” And then His Honor went
off to stew in his own gravy.
That the Dean case will yet be thoroughly re-
viewed there can be no manner of doubt. And
the most interesting part of the new hearing,
whatever form it takes, will (provided no
arsenic is meanwhile traced to prisoner’s posses-
sion) be the medical evidence. The most intelli-
gent of tbe profession seem unanimous in the
opinion that, whatever Mrs, Dean’s present condi-
tion, there never was even a reasonably-conclusive
prima-facie case against the accused.
• * •
The foreman of the jury in the Dean case
hardly deserves all the obloquy that has been
heaped upon him. His wife was only confined a
day or two before the trial, and his eldest son
underwent a critical surgical operation, which re-
sulted fatally, while he was actually in the
jury-box. It is not to be supposed that any man
would, under the circumstances, be able to calmly
weigh evidence or patiently view the prospect of
being further locked up for two nights and a day.
Under such circumstances, hurried verdicts,
founded on illogical compromises, are not to be
wondered at.
• « •
The attitude of Windeyer, J., and the
verdict of the jury, in the Dean trial, like the
phenomena lately produced by the spiritualist ;
medium, Mrs. Mellon, in the presence of his
Honor, are, to quote the words of the report
signed by Windeyer, J., aforesaid, as a member of
the manifestations-investigation committee, “ im-
possible to account for by any of the known laws
of Nature, as at present understood.”

Pearl of wisdom by J. Hogue, M.P. (N.S.
Wales:—“lf he (Dean) had attempted to poison the
mother-m-law we could have understood it, and
there would have been some rational theory to
base his guilt upon. But that he should poison
his own wife. ...” A man ought usually to
begin with his mother-in-law, anyhow, though
there may sometimes be arguments in favor of
practising on your wife so as to make quite sure of
her ma afterwards.

The Dean case reminds The Bulletin
roundaboutly of the (Victorian) Spoarin case.
Dear Bulletin, — Is it not a fact (tet
toxicological experts say) that habitual arsenic-
eaters—and there are many females who take
arsenic regularly in order to achieve a fat,
pasty, fair complexion—can take what are to
others poisonous doses daily without apparent
ill results; until, suddenly, at some other weak
turn of health, and especially if they cease the
daily dose they suddenly exhibit severe symptoms
of arsenical poisoning?— DlPSO.
» * #
Aii ancient Sydney landmark has just
disappeared—the Phillip-street oak. It was grown
from an acorn brought from Essex by a “patriot”
named Solomon, who planted it in 1801. The
pulling-down of the house in front of which the
tree stood necessitated its uprooting. The house
in question was built by “Dicky” Driver’s
grandfather in 1798, of the real, original “ Brick-
fields’’clay. There Dicky’s father was born and
lived till he went into the old pub., famous in
Sydney annals, at King-street corner. The
site of the cottage at this date is about
the only spot anywhere near the centre of
Sydney where the original conformation of the
ground can be seen. The preservation of the
tree for so long after it encroached upon the
footpath was due to a regard for it on the part of
Mr. John Starkey, owner of the adjacent pro-
perty, who must have paid pretty dearly for his
sentiment in the loss of rent. The oak is shown
in a drawing of Sydney of 1810 as a sapling. It
was at one time used as a watch-post—the limit
of the patrolmen’s beat—and a company of
soldiers marching to St. James’ one Sunday in
1815, along what was then the ridge of the road,
was bogged opposite to it. The men were told
“ to break ranks ” and “form by the tree,” when
it was seen that several had left their shoes in the
mud. Colonel Molle, “the domineering brute”
of Wentworth’s satire, was present, and sent the
men to church shoeless.

S.A. teetotallers are indignant over “Sir
Buxton’s” appointment as Governor. The
Southern Baptist, an Adelaide sheet, describes
him as coming from a family “made by beer.”
“ How can we associate with a man,” says a
prominent Ad. teetotaller, “ who made his money
out of the blood of thousands of the poorer
classes?”
There are two Adelaide citizens who know Gov.
Buxton “well,” but they differ in description.
One describes him as “a tall, thin man, with
mutton-chop whiskers,” and another has limned
him as “of medium height, stout, dignified
demeanor, and with a magnificent beard.” When
Buxton arrives he will probably turn out to be a
pompous little man, sft. high, with a balloon-like
stomach, and the usual evangelical upper-lip.
# # #
Lord Brassey is described by a Melbourne
Herald correspondent as a very unattractive
speaker. “He is the great antithesis of the light
and graceful style of Lord Hopetoun.” Hardlyas
bad as that, surely ; but The Bulletin rejoices to
know that Melbourne banquet-snappers and
bibulous dead-heads will, in future, have to suffer
more and more for their iniquities. One hopes
“the groaning bored ” will be a literally correct
expression in the journalistic reports of future
State gorges and guzzles in the marvellous city.

One of the presents to Swilliam Clarke’s
daughter on the occasion of her recent marriage to
Mr. Cruickshank, was a box of butter, from a
local dairyman. One could have understood this
better if it had happened at the recent Chirn-side
wedding.

The Martial-Law Courtand the N.S.W. Court
of Executive cancelled one another in the Captain
Close case, and now the N.S.W. Divorce Court
and the Court of Ecclesiastical Jurisdiction have
cancelled one another in the Rev. James Clarke
case. So much fur the finality of law.

THE N.S.W. WHITE ELEPHANT .”
PREMIER REID: “I think I'll send him to
the Zoo!”
In one week Chief Justice Griffith in the
South and Judge Noel in the North of Queensland
told juries that they were scandals to the adminis-
tration of law. In one case the jury would not
agree, in the other it would not convict. Is there
any record in any part of Australia of a Judge
blackguarding a jury for bringing an accused in
guilty of any offence, especially a hangable one?
* # •
Queensland has six Kanakas under sen-
tence of death for a cold-blooded murder of an
old hawker near Bundaberg. The sugar-planter
papers shriek for the blood of the savages, but, at
the same time, point out that they come from
Malayta, where murder is not regarded as a
serious crime, Queensland, in importing murder-
ous savages, should be prepared to take all re-
sponsibility. The Govt, is morally guilty of the
manslaughter—at all events—of the old pedlar,
for it permitted the importation of men known
to be cannibalistic savages of the fiercest type.
By the way, Bundaberg (Q.) Mail remarks, re
the proposed slaughter: “It is, under the
exigencies of the position, almost imperative that
the men be hanged in Bundaberg.” Bundaberg
always was ambitious.

In Wesfcralia the “ Japs ” are driving poor
women from their wash-tubs; Afghans have
control of three-fourths of the carrying trade to
the goldfields; and Chinese are out-doing the
Europeans at shopkeeping.
* * •
The Chinese 'washerman has now almost
got into Bourke-st., Melb. To overcome the
prejudices of people who object to John doing up
their linen—John employs a Mr. (say) Brown-
Jobson to open a nice shop with “English
Laundry ” over the door. John himself is never
seen near the place. Every nationality is now
represented by laundries in Melb. —but the Irish.
Mrs. P. Murphy is still only a washerwoman.
* * *
The Rev. Rentoul (Melb.) is of opinion
that bicycle-riding “purifies a girl’s spirit.” A
case in point : There is a peaceful old crank in
Sydney whose occupation seems to consist in
removing refuse out of a stable, and his barrow
bears the inscription, “God is love.” The other
day he shambled out into the street with his
barrow and his inscription,
and the wheel was just in time
to upset a lady cyclist on her
back in the gutter, with her
feet and two clocked stockings
flying in the air. And when
she collected herself out of the
dust, and felt for her back hair,
and recovered her lost hat, she
helped the old person to right
his barrow, which had gone
over }n the collision, and then
rode off without using a single
strong expression. She went
away with a bright smile on
her countenance, and the old
man stood gaping with his
barrow of refuse and obviously
fell in love with her on the
spot.

Editor Bulletin,— He was commercial
editor of a working-man’s newspaper, where the
“ghost” was ghostly, and he told me that his
penury had compelled him to forego sustenance
altogether for nearly two days. He was quali-
fying for an angel. All that time the commercial-
man had been informing the public that flour was
rising, but butter had gone down. Grim joke,
wasn’t it? —T.D.

Mr. F. B. Sutbor and family are about to
take up permanent residence at Katella homestead,
Macquarie River, near Wellington, their residence,
Bradwardine, near Bathurst, having been leased
for a term. At Bradwardine, in Gov, Loftua’
time, took place the only Executive Council meet-
ing ever held outside Sydney.
# * *
Card. Moran favors the establishment of
a “ Eaugh-a-ballagh Regiment ”in N.S. W. The
scheme is, perhaps the reactionary result of fore-
going the usual St. Patrick’s Day whisky this
year, merely because March 17 happened on a
Sunday. Which shows the folly of neglecting to
take one’s “ medsin ” at the proper interval.
Australia doesn’t want to breed any Scotch, Irish
or English regiments. The next generation should
be “ Australian ” only.

Pneumatic boots!
An eager man rushed
Into Foy’s (Melb.), the
other day, to offer a new
patent, for £5OO 0.
“There’s millions in it,”
be cried. To walk on air
is no longer a poetic fie-
tion! Pump air into your
pumps and waltz over
creation!” Managers
heard the inventor—they
came to scoff and stayed
to contemplate. The
boots were there, in fact.
Theinventorused a pump
at the instep and the soles
became thicker, and the
heels, which had been
crushed like a concertina,
assumed a normal shape.
Managers put on the
boots and walked and
wondered. Pliancy,light-
ness, silence—all these
the inventor claimed uu-
contradicted. But a
severer test was needed.
How would they suit a
15-stone man. “Bring
him here and try,’’said
the inventor. They
brought John May, the
colos.'us of Foy’s. The
circular John blushed
when they called him a
15-stone man. “ I’m
17f-stone,” he said. A 1
the better. The inventor
pumped the boots tighter
and handed them over.
John walked therein and
said he liked them foine.
So it goes on. But,
when the pneumatic
fiends have used up all
the air on boots, bikes,
pea-rifles, and parlia-
ment, what is the popu-
lation going to do for
breath ?

Rockhampton stole
from Malta the allitera-
tion which justifies its
proud title of the “City
of Three S’s.” Malta’s
capital, Yaletta, has long
been known as “the city
of sin, sweat, steps and
sorrow.” There is no-
thing new under the sun.
A meeting was held in Sydney the othir
d:iy with a view to establishing a “Clerks and
Warehousemen’s Benefit Association.” The Asso-
ciation is to consist of employes only, and its
benefit funds are to be subscribed by employes
only. Nevertheless, the obsequious clerks ami
warehousemen invited all the leading employers i f
Sydney to attend, and they occupied the platform,
while the clerks and warehousemen filled the body
of the hall. An employer was in the chair, and
the officers elected were all employers, except
Chief Justice Darley, who condescendingly ac-
cepted the position of patron, his letter to that
effect being read amid the enthusiastic applause of
the clerks and warehousemen. The billet of trea-
surer is not yet filled, but a bank manager is to
have it later on. There is a fine element of
grovel about the clerks and warehousemen. No
other men would refuse to trust themselves with
their own money, and ask their bosses to take
care of it, and do as they darned well
liked with it; or would refuse to manage their
own association; or would think of having a
“patron ” hitched on to a purely business under-
taking. The patron, by the way, has nothing to
do and doesn’t subscribe or assist in any way ;■ he
is wanted solely for prostrative purposes, and why
the Emperor of Germany wasn’t offered the billet
is inexplicable. Also, the clerks and warehouse-
men are the only people The Bulletin knows of
who, when they met to start a plain business un-
dertaking with their own money and for their own
benefit, would spend most of the time cheering
their employers and the Chief Justice. Walter
Besant once said that there was nothing else on
this earth quite so hopeless and abject as the
cheap clerk. Which statement only goes to show
that Besant hasn’t studied the warehouseman.

Judging by the report of a Board of Health
officer on the typhoid plague in the Sydney suburb
of Erskineville—a place chiefly jerry-built on
ground filled up with dust-cart rubbish and gar-
bage, the proper treatment would be to set fire to
the whole suburb, burn it to the ground, take up
the improperly-constructed sewers and relay them
properly, fine most of the burnt-out landlords
heavily, or, in default, confiscate their land, and
start afresh.

Miss Maud Hobson has been much admired
in Melb. streets in a Tam O’Shanter hat of green
velvet, worn jauntily on the side of her pretty
head, and showing off her beautiful fair hair to
the utmost advantage ; and a brown costume with
green velvet bodice to match the hat. Passing up
Oollins-street at the busiest time of the day, when
the pavement outside the Stock Exchange was
crowded with brokers just come off Change, she
found a way cleared for her through the tluong,
and busy brokers leaving off their bartering to
mutter beneath their breath, in tones of reverent
rapture, that yonder was the Gaiety girl who was
lorn at St. Kilda. Maud is inclined to stoutness
and her waist is ample. [This has evidently
strayed out of the advt. columns. Ed. B.j

The erstwhile proprietor ot a Sydney ex-
press-messenger-despatch concern of over 30 years
ago tells a story which should serve as a special
warning to all women with a past, and to diary-
keepers in particular. In the east of the city
there resided a mysterious single lady with her
alleged nephew, a little boy, who used to mind the
house whenever “auntie” went out. She was
very reserved, had few visitors, and, never mixing
with her neighbors, was generally
unpopular with the female con-
tingent around. One afternoon
some express-messenger boys de-
livered a draper’s circular from
house to house in her street, much
to the admiration of the young-
ster, who resolved to emulate
their style and speed. When
the aunt came home that evening
nearly every woman was at her
gate and passed some
remarkably scathing
observation as she
went by. “ Your
little son has just
gone home,” volun-
teered one lady.
“Your own loving
pet,” tittered a bony
female from her
verandah. “ When
you last kissed me, dar-
ling, Iswore nothing should
part us,” shouted a giddy
miss as she ran in and slara-
-*J*med the door. “How’s
Georgie?” enquired another.
Reaching home at last with
rage and bewilderment in her
heart, she discovered a horrible
thing. The youngster had removed a
packet of old letters from the chest of
drawers and carefully deposited an
epistle under the door of every house-
holder in the street. He hadn’t missed
one. It was the- most conscientious
non-professional delivery on record.
Sam Lyons, Premier Turner’s senior
partner, the brisk attorney, told the Fitzroy
bench, the other day, that, as a member of the
School Board of Advice, he found the girls far
more addicted than the boys to writing indelicacies
on the walls.

In the above portrait The Bulletin tries
to give its readers some idea of the loveliness ot
the ruling Melb. beauty. Mechanical processes,
alas! play havoc with “the exquisite coloring,
the rose-leaf tint of the skin, the deep blue of the
eyes, and the changing brown and gold of the sott
hair,” which have made her for the past two
seasons belle of the Southern city. “ Yet (writes
the enthusiastic slave whose_ soul is translated
into the foregoing epithets) “if but a tithe of her
charms be reproduced, her sway will at once be
acknowledged.” The lady is married, has a sturdy
two-year-old son, and is the daughter of a well-
known Melb. medico.

The absence of clean-shaved, long-haired,
low-collared and daintily effeminate young men
from Melbourne Block during the last few
weeks is almost as remarkable as the rush the
barbers have had for moustache-producers and
close crops. More than one Melb. hosiery-house
has been almost cleaned out of stand-up collars
and the pawnbrokers absolutely refuse to advance
on velvet coats. Change of fashion, Ihk Bulle-
tin wildly imagines.

A Koroit (Vic.) widow last week reported
herself as the victim of a criminal assault. The
police investigated the matter, traced up suspected
persons, and no doubt would soon have found
evidence against some unfortunate man, when the
woman wrote and explained that it was all a
nightmare. How many men are now in gaol for
less? It recalls a mot: “Why do old maids
look under the beds every night ?” ‘lor tear
there may be a man there.” “No ; in the hope
that there may be a man there.

Cabled that Lord Sholto Douglas, son of
that queer person the Marquis of Queensberry,
has been arrested in California “on a charge
of insanity, in order to prevent his mar-
riage with a waitress.” The waitress s friends
doubtless preferred to run any risk rather than
that of contamination by the English nobility.
The Douglases are a cranky lot. One of the Mar-
quis’s sisters married a journeyman baker,
another sister, Lady Florence Dixie, immortalised
herself by her hysterical romance about 1 enians
in buckram ; and another son of the Marquis,
Lord Alfred, is the friend of Oscar Wilde.
• * *
Lord Howe Island (a dependency of N.S.
Wales) does not contain a woman who is 25 years
old and unmarried. The maidens from 18 to 26
are the real rulers of the island. Better to start
life as a doorstep foundling than as an unprotected
male in Lord Howe Island, where female suffrage
would be a superfluous calamity.

G F. : This paper doesn’t recommend any English gram-
mar The flrst to hand will do for reference ; none, to
the general student, is worth study for itself—none ade-
quate, complete, up-to-date. Grammars are the Pro
crustean beds of language; the Chinese flower-pots which
stunt a noble tree. The time spent over them in schools
is mainly wasted—just as if the scholars were to pore all
the year round over the Marylebone rules in Ihe hope of
learning how to play cricket. If you want to write
English, read English—get familiar with evety noble
stroke in the game, and knowledge of the rules will come
of itself. Study, if you like, the history and structure of
the language in books which bring brains to illuminat e
the mechanism (Meiklejohn’s “ English Language,
through Book Exchange, Is. 3d., is a jewel). But mere
collections of arbitrary rules are almost useless. There is
nothing arbitrary in language, which changes from day
to day. The momentary usage of the majority is the only
criterion of correctness. If there is one thing the gram-
mars are clear about, it is that plural isn’t singular, yet,
almost within the memory of grammars, the plural
“you” has come to refer to a single person, and now
young Australia pluralises it as “yous.” At present,
“yous” is wrong; but when, if ever, a majority of Aus-
tralians adopt it, and it wins a footing in classical litera-
ture, it will be right.

who will be the public's favorite ere the season is done,
has a distinct leaning towards legitimate drama, ami to
that end she makes every effort to remove all elocutionary
trace of her slight and charming Austrian accent. H-r
graceful carriage, which has so captivated Melh. johnnie-
doni, is the outcome of education at a fjeipsic school,
where physical deportment was ranked as an essential in
girlish training.

Uanies R. Anderson died in England a few weeks ago,
aged 84. He acted as a leading tragedian for half-a-cen-
tury. One of Macready's right-hand men. Came to Aus-
tralia, hut. failed, through the superior lustre of the
younger Montgomery.
Prof. Marshall Hall dedicates his new overture to Gior-
dano Bruno, the atheist martyr burnt at Rome. It is to
he called the Bruno overture. The memory of Bruno is
kept green by Art. A statue to him has recently been
erected in Rome; Swinburne has devoted two soiunts
to him in his latest volume ; and now Marshall Hall calls
an overture after his name. But what do the Melb. unco
(ftii I say to the musical professor of their University dedi-
cating an overture to a roasted heretic?

Many ladies who favor that most becoming
fashion of tailor-made gowns have lost sight of the
King of Ladies’ Tailors in Australasia—Mr. B.
King, late of Coil ins-street, and, we are sure, will
be pleased to discover him again. We are able to
give the information. We now find him with
Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson and Sons, the successors
of the old-established firm, Moubray, Rowan and
Hicks, who, under his guidance, have made a
special department for Ladies’Tailoring—a branch
which it is not surprising to see daily increases in
favor. It is to Mr. King that ladies have to be
grateful for a new patent riding-skirt, which
minimises the danger of a fall.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
That smart Melb. cricketer, Frank Walters,
takes up his residence in Sydney shortly to qualify for the
N.S. \V. eleven next season. He yearns for revenue on the
Vic. Association for what lie considers harsh and un-
merited treatment; and aspires to follow Percy M'Domnll's
example of leaving Victoria and scoring a few double-
century innings against it.

• ♦ * *
There is little enthusiasm displayed over the
opening of Melb. football season. The strictly amateur
champion footballer, who loafed about in piths, from one
year s end to the otln r, and who always seemed flush,
now finds his occupation gone—also his free beers—and
the incense of harrackers’ admiration is no longer wafted
up his nostrils. Those snug lit tie billets of keeping tne
sparrows off the cricket-pilch, at £2 10s. per week, are no
more, and footballers now have to work for their living
like ordinary mortals.
* * ♦ ♦
Of all the English cricketers who have visited
Australia, how many have made their homes here? Charley
Lawrence settled in a cosy Govt, billet at Newcastle,
where he still resides. ■ Caffyn, of the same team, carried
on a hair-dressing business in George-st reet, Sydney, fora
few years, but returned to England in the seventies.

Mr. A. W. Bales won the big haivhcap and the title of
champion pigeon shot of Australia, with 50 successive
kills. Dr. Dttflnt won the Bracelet competition with 35
successive kills, Mr. Bales running second with 34. The
cartridges which both cracks used were supple d to them
by H. R. L-aoh, the popular Sydney gunsmith and spoi ts-
ma i. Perhaps the m st precocious pigeon-shot in N.S.
Wales is a boy seven years old, who mes one of Leach’s
small “collecting” guns, and has won an open pigeon-
match, killing six birds out of seven.

J. E. Snell, the cyclist who put up a wonderful
record between Adelaide and Melh, last year, recently did
245 miles under 24 hours, thus heating Duff, of Maoriland,
who held the record with 227 miles in the same time.
Soell is a poor racer, hut breaks records with ease and
seems to possess unlimited endurance.

Felix, if liia contest with Dooley results favor-
ably* means to go to England, where tie may have a

It is now stated that whoever happens to draw
the Trenton colt, Trentliam, in St. Albans* lottery, will
probably draw the winner of the next Derby. He is said
to he a cut above either Wallace or Challenger.
* • * *
At Elaine (Vic.) races, some of the stewards
showed marked independence of V.R.C. rules. Darby
started at 6 to 4 on, and Freestone at even money, forth"
Ponv Rice. Freestone won easily, and Darby’s owner
protested-that the race was not the stipulated distance.
Consideration of the protest was deferred ; the races were
finished, and the officials, except two or three stewards,
left the course. The remaining stewards then dealt with
the protest, and, though Darby was the only competing
pony still on the ground, decided that the race should be
run again. A temporary judge and starter were ap-
pointed, and, having covered the ground solus, Darby was
declared the winner. The owner of Freestone threatens
legal proceedings.
* ♦ » *
The two country speculators who bought the
gates at Onkaparinga rams on Easter Monday, paying
£476 for the privilege of collecting entrance money, were
staggered at the tricks which hundreds of “patrons”
tried to play. City swells on drags and coaches worked
the “ Oh, we ate memhahs ” dodge. Jockeys engaged in
the meeting were admitted free on presenting their certi-
ficates ; and over 200 such tickets w-ere handed in, repre-
senting about six times the number of actual riders. The
“ have ” wasn’t detected at the time, but the gaternen
bucked when a woman came up smiling with a Jock.’s
ticket and, being challenged, blandly said she was “ a
trainer." Over 500 people got in without paying, and
yet the Mt. Barker partners made £l5O profit out of their
risky investment.

Another totalisator argument. At Onkaparinga
(S.A.) races on Easter Monday £18,846 passed through the
totalisaior, a record for S.A. The club thus makes about
£l5OO by the machine alone, and the public, moreover, got
better odds all day from the machine than from the books
one horse paving £44 div. In the old days the books
would have taken what now goes to the clubs and is spent
in improving the accommodation for the public and hltwed
it in buying a broad-gauge suit and a diamond pin.

Banking.
The Colonial Hank of Australasia

The FOURTH REPORT of the DIRECTORS
Of
The COLONIAL BANK of AUSTRALASIA
LIMITED,
To be Presented to the Shareholders at the Fourth
Ordinary General Meeting, to be Held at the
Bank, 126 Elizabeth-street, at noon on Tuesday,
30th April, 1895.
REPORT.
The directors beg to submit to the shareholders
their fourth report with a balance-sheet and state-
ment of profit and loss for the half-year ended the
31sl March, duly audited.
In consequence of the shrinkage in the volume
of business, the low rates ruling for money, ami
the high rate of interest payable on deferred
deposits, the directors regret jihat the transactions
fur tiie half-year, after payment of interest on
deposits and expenses, show that the balance at
credit of profit and loss brought forward from last
half-year, viz., <£4937 18s. lid., has been reduced
to <£243 25., which will be carried forward to next
half-year.
As it is obvious that the bank cannot continue
to pay the high rate of per cent, per annum on
its long-dated deposits, your directors will endea-
vour at an early date to obtain the consent of the
deferred deposit receipt holders to a reduction in
the rate of interest.
The market for securities is still in a stagnant
state, and although it is impossible to value them
accurately we feel that when the proposed amend-
ment of the Companies Act is passed it will be
necessary to.make further provision for losses by
writing off a portion of the capital. _
Four branches have, been closed during the half-
year, viz., North IMolbourne, Sauci-
ford, and Orbost.
Your directors have given special attention to
the expenditure, of the bank, ar.d it will be
that during the half-year a reduction of
6s. Iftd. has been made in the expenses, in addi-
tion to previous reductions. ,
The fourth ordinary general meeting of share-
holders will be held at the head office of the com-
pany, 126 Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, on 1 ues-
day, the 30th day of April, 1895, at noon.
By order of the board.
SELBY PAXTON,
General Manager.
Melbourne, 23rd April, 1895.
The COLONIAL BANK of AUSTRALASIA
LIMITED.

The last words of jockey Jimmy Hayes ere
leaving for ’Frisco were, “Save me from my friends!”
He had, he said, resolved that he would never again in
his life allow himself to be influenced and controlled by
pals,” considering that the early and ignominious close
of his Australian career was caused by placing too much
confidence in people who were undeserving of it. On the
same occasion Hayes stated that Stevenson “ has the best
bands of any jockey now riding.” And Hayes is not a
bad judge of riding. , m ,

Mayor Lees “firmly but courteously
declined” to preside at the big Sydney Centen-
nial Hall meeting on the Dean case. Mr. Lees
thus tirmly but courteously declined to do his
duty as Mayor. His alleged reason seems to be
that be doesn’t like to aid in bringing pressure on
Bench or Executive. Then why did he go on the
recent deputation to urge Abigail’s release?
The trustees of N.S.W, Art Gallery have
consented to act as judges in an art competition
for prizes of 75, 25, and 15gs., offered by Mr. H.
S. Chipman for tiie three best pictures of
“ Minerva, the Goddess of Wisdom,” the original
work of Australasian artists. The prizes are
sufficiently liberal to tempt considerable compe-
tition, and there should be a collection worth
seeing at Sydney Art Gallery on Ist Sept, next,
when the competition closes.
The recent fatal fire at Parkes (N.S.W.),
when two women and five children lo.t their lives,
is a solemn warning to the woman who suffers
from chronic burglar scare. The deceased
lived in constant dread of that anticipated
li .use-breaker who never comes except when
he is not expected, and, in order to keep
hmi out, they had all the windows nailed up and
the doors secured with a multitude of elaborate
f.jBtenihgfcr - When the fire broke out they conldn t
undo the fastenings or burst the casements, and
the n itural result ensued. A nail in a window-
b tsh is very-little good to keep a burglar out, but
for keeping-two terrified women and five children
in it h invaluable.
“Healthy Mothers and Sturdy Children :
the Australian Wife’s Book,” published by E. W.
Cole, Melbourne, is a bandy compendium of medi-
cal science and common sense, especially designed
for the u&e of families. The work has been com-
piled with great care, and should prove a boon to
every house-mother, but particularly to her whose
lot is cast in the Australian bush.
Toxa, the new rabbit destroyer, is making
great headway. It is incombustible, in which
respect it has a great advantage over all com-
pounds containing phosphorus; it is cheap,
efficacious, and as horses, cattle, and sheep refuse
to eat it, while the rabbit takes it freely, it sticks
closely to business. Half-a-crowu’s worth of
Toxa is reported, on apparently good authority,
to have killed 550 rabbits, and one large run in
Maoriland has been so effectually cleared that it
now carries 30,000 more sheep than formerly.
Jules Renard & Co., of 306 Kent-street, are the
Sydney agents.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.
Nathan Herman, whose Misfit Parlor in
Wynyard-street is the beginning of a new Sydney
industry, finds that he has opened up a larger
industry than he was aware of. There are more
misfit boots, shirts, dresses and clothes generally
than he imagined in his wildest dreams, and the
immense quantity which he has bought up enables
him now to fit everybody at the lowest possible
rates, except the individual of whom there is only
one sample in all the world. And even he may be
fitted byaccident. Mr. Herman is going m for
the business of a universal provider, and though
lie isn’t quite universal he has got a consider-
able distance on the way. There are 20 separate
departments in the Wynyard-street warehouse,
including glass, crockery, bedding, bedsteads,
carpets, watches, niauioiicls, ulaukets,
boys’ clothing, men’s clothing, ladies’ clothing,
and many other kinds of clothing. Mr. Herman
also supplies excellent cigars (not misfits) at IM.
each, and the gaudy Masonic apron, which the
lodge furnishes with awe-inspiring ceremonial at
245., he supplies at 7s. 6d. Most of the awful
majesty is gone from the great Masonic bosh
when the apron can be bought for /s. od.
Mr. Herman sends all kinds of goods free, in
large or small quantities, to any part of N.o.
Wales, but he whoops most particularly about
his £2 parcel, which consists of two shirts, boiled
or colored as required, two undershirts six
pairs socks, two tics, two collars, six hand-
kerchiefs, one set studs, one hat, one pair
braces, a suit of clothes, and a pair of
boots. This parcel is the great effort of his life,
and anyone sending along the £2 will get it with
all possible despatch. Any person who is afraid
to send along the £2 without a reference, is re-
quested to inquire of the Hank of N.S.W. At
present the premises are being enlarged to make
room for an unlooked-for rush of business, and a
gilded minion at the front door is kept busy
pointing out the way to the department where
the waterproof coat is sold at Ids- bd.

The FOURTH REPORT of the DIRECTORS of the NATIONAL BANK OF AUSTRALASIA
LIMITED.
The directors regret to have to intimate to the shareholders tliat the result of the business of the
bank for the half-year to 31st March last does not justify the declaration of a dividend on the ordinary
shares on the present occasion.
The net profit of the period, after deducting expenses of management, interest paid and accrued
on deposits, rebate, etc., amounted t 0... ... ... ... ... .£17,710 11 3

Shareholders will observe that contraction in the office expenditure still continues.
During the half-year the paid-up capital has been increased by <£87,233 12s. Bd., the total standing
at 451,777,784 15s. 4d.
Tha branches at Albert-park, Clones, Newlyn and Yarraville, in Victoria, have been closed.
The ORDINARY GENERAL MEETING will be held on WEDNESDAY, Ist MAY, at noon,
at the Head Office, Coll ins-street, Melbourne.
In terms of the articles of association, the Honble. William Austin Zeal, M.L.C., and the Honble.

present occupants of the office are Mr. W. H. Tuckett and Mr. Andrew Burns.

The climax of the tissue of illegalities and
follies attending the court-martial on the case of
Captain Close, of N.S. W. Artillery, was reached
when the Executive undertook to review the find-
ings of the Court. They could no more refuse to
“confirm” the verdict of "guilty” on the 3rd
and 4th charges than they could reverse the ver-
dict of a jury. They could send the case back to
be reconsidered, or they could remit the punish-
ment ; but the verdict must .stand, rightly or
wrongly, as one of “guilty.” All the same, the
whole of the proceedings, from first to last, were
grossly irregular—the composition of the Court
included.
The strike at Hudson Bros.’, Sydney, has
ended in a o >mpromise which, from thecommunity’s
pomt of view, is only less injurious than the firm’s
original proposals. The employers, who pre-
viously paid avages 10 per cent, below ordinary
tradej’ates, wished to make a further reduction of
10, 15, and 20 per cent. The employes have
agreed to a further reduction of 7, 10, and per
cent. Therefore, if there is any force in the
logic and rhetoric which this paper has brought
to bear on the question, the wages of com-
petitive firms’ employees must sooner or later
suffer a similar reduction. The men, in fact,
have given away the whole principle which justi-
fied their strike—the only principle on which the
“ law of supply and demand ” can be effectively
attacked. Only, they have not given it away
quite so violently as they were asked to do.
Would The Bulletin, then, have had the men
continue on strike ? There is no incumbency to
answer the question. This paper pointed out the
right course ; but on the men themselves must rest
the onus of deciding its feasibility in the face of
starvation.

Toro: “Re bull savagery. I think the
bull’s ferocity increases with the cold of the
climate. Anyone who has seen a Spanish bull-
fight will testify how humble the bulls are, in
that warm country, and what a lot of prodding
they take from the lances of the picadores. This
even with the so-called fierce black bulls of
Andalusia, which are not a patch on a real John
Bull—say one from the Highlands.”
Dear Bulletin, — Rev. Haweis’s idea of
the hotel-church is realised at Wyalong, N.S.W.,
where the Baptists meet on Sundays in a local
pub—the holy and unholy spirit seeming to frater-
nise very amicably. Whereupon, I ask you : (1)
How does this fit in with the Licensing Act,
which prohibits hotel entertainments, especially
on Sundays? Is a religious corrobboree an “ en-
tertainmentwithin the meaning of the Act? (2)
What defence could publicans make to a charge of
selling watered whisky, with their houses filled
with the emanations of an aquatic creed ? (3)
Where does that celebrated injunction come in,
“Don’t mix ? Hardshell.

Once a Bishop of Riverina was being
driven in a buggy by the station black-boy. On
the way they encountered a gate. The black-boy
drew up the horses; the bishop looked at the
black-boy, who took the hint, handed the reins to
the man of prayer, jumped down, and opened the
gate. Later another gate obstructed. The bishop
looked at the black-boy ; the black-boy looked at
the bishop. The bishop said: “My boy, please
get down and open the gate.” The black-boy
obeyed. Soon they reached a third gate, and as
the black-boy was reining in the horses he said :
“ Baal open this feller blanky gate, Bishop; your
turn now !” The bishop complied.

In the Greener-Abrahams (civil) case the
other day, at’Melbourne, Emanuel Abrahams, in
cross-examination, told Mr. Purves, Q.C., that he
did not consider him honest, as he had accepted a
retainer and eight guineas from 'them, and was
now appearing against them. Whereupon the
genial Purves_ explained that the retainer was only
a general retainer to defend them at the Criminal
Court, and -‘at the Criminal Court he would give
them the value of their money as far as he possibly
could.”

THIS Company is formed to acquire Fifteen auriferous sections, immediately adjacent to tlie cele-
brated Harris’ Reward Gold Mine, at Mount Jukes, West Tasmania, and to work or re-sell all
or any of the same. The sections are known or numbered as follows on the accompanying plan of the
district: —Nos. 7 (otherwise Sailor Jack), 9, 12, 14, 34, 36, 39, 44, and A, B, C, D, E, F and G. The
sections comprise an area of about 140 acres.
As will be seen from the plan, what is believed to be Harris’ Reward rich reef, has been found on
section No. 9. An auriferous reef has also been located on section B, whilst a third reef is proved on
section No. 7 (Sailor Jack).
The developments now taking place on Harris’ Reward Claim prove the existence of an enormous
body of highly ptyable quartz, the reef being from 8 to 12 feet wide, and in the opinion of the Manager
(Mr. Nugent) good for 6to 7oz. per ton. In his report of 16th April on the Harris claim, Mr. Nugent
states :*' I have extended Harris’ trench 6 feet further South. There is a splendid show in it. The
reef is 12 feet wide, and every stone a good specimen. I sunk a shaft 28 feet deep on the Western
wall, at the extreme Northern end. There is a grand reef in sight, very rich, and a 6-iuoh leader
extremely rich.
It is odieved that every one of the sections proposed to be acquired by the Company will prove
aunfi io is.
S • i nportaut are the developments on the Harris Reward Mine (which was only discovered and
taken up last N w Year’s Day) considered by the Government of Tasmania that despite the depleted
condition of the Treasury, the Government are constructing a road thereto at a cost of <£460—,£300 to
be paid by the Government of Tasmania, £5O by the Harris’ Reward Gold Mine, and £lOO by this
proprietary.
The intention of the proprietary is, as developments proceed on the Harris’ Reward, and on the
sections belonging to the proprietary, to re-sell to subsidiary Companies on favorable terms a portion
or al of the propriet ir y sections. It will easily be understood that a considerable profit may be
derived from the re-sale of these sections.
There is no doubt that the rich alluvial gold, worked in the immediate neighbourhood of Mount
Juk es, was derived from the line of reef traversing these sections.
As an evidence of their belief in the mine, the Vendors are content to take a large portion of the
purchase consideration in contributing and other shares.
If no allotment, the purchase money will be returned in full; in the case of allotted shares, if the
allotment-money is not paid within eight days of the allotment, the allotment may be cancelled, and
the application deposit forfeited to the Company.
Reports, specimens, and plans are on view at the Company’s offices, and a careful inspection ot
these is confidently invited. A copy of a report, also, by A. T. Kenney, the well-known mining
expert, is appended. r
Report by A. T. Kenney, on Mount Jukes Proprietary Sections.
W. H. DICKSON, Esq.,
South Yarra, April 16th, 1895.
SIR.—In compliance with the request embodied in yours to hand of the 9th inst., lam pleased to
state that during a four weeks’visit of inspection to Harris’ Reward claim and other leases on Mt
Jukes, West Tasmania, I examined Blocks Nos. 7,9, 12, 14, 34, 36, 39, 44, and Blocks marked ABC
D, E, F, G, on accompanying plan, which are now under offer for flotation, and report thereon as
follows :
1. Blocks Nos. 36, A, 9 and 12 are North to North West of, and adjoining, Harris’ Reward Claim
and directly on the line of Harris’ rich reef, which has been traced and proved on Block No 9 carry-
ing gold. * ’ J
2. Block B.—ln addition to the probability of getting the underlay of Harris’ rich reef in depth
a Separate auriferous reef has been found on the South East corner of this lease.
3. Blocks Nos. 14, 34 and 44 lie West to South West of Harris’ Reward claim. As the reef in
Harris’ Reward claim underlays West, these blocks have good prospects of cutting same, and are con-
sequently very valuable.
4. Blocks 39, D, F and G are on the direct line of Harris’ Reward rich reef, and the surface stone
showing gold freely can be traced on the surface at intervals through them.
5. Block 7, known as Sailor Jack.—An underlay shaft has been sunk 40 feet, the stone being well
mineralised, assays taken from this reef giving good prospects of gold and silver. This is a parallel
lode to and quite distinct from Harris’ Reward reef. 1
6. 0 and E (West of 39, D and F) promise well for the West underlay of Harris’ Reward
rich reef.
In conclusion, considering the wonderful character of the developments so far on the Reward
claim, I consider your proprietary have secured in these sections a most valuable property posses-ing
promise of highly remunerative returns to parties investing in them. ’ ' 9
I am. Sir, yours faithfully, KENNEY
