N.T.’s Empty Bottles.
The Northern Territory (S. A.) Times prints the follow-
ing verses, read by an unnamed member of the Palmerston
Literary and Debating Society at a recent meeting. Who-
ever he may be, that man has a fine eye for a point and a
moral; and The Bulletin wants to hear from him again.
Bottles everywhere just teeming, lying all about
and seeming
To be ever, ever dreaming of the days that are no
more;
And I camped each night a rover, as I trod the
country over,
Hardly finding room to lay me for them as I
sought the shore,
Each day finding as I sought Australia’s farthest
northern shore,
Empty bottles on before.
On the burning plains they’re scattered, and
where miners rocks have shattered,
And on stations, and ’way inland by the deep
artesian bore,
Whence we all too often borrow ghastly tales of
grief and sorrow;
Grief of those who know no morrow, ending life
in blood and gore.
But what made their lives so precious shudder out
in blood and gore ?
Empty bottles, nothing more.
’Neath the darksome jungle’s shutter, where the
wood-doves flirt and flutter,
Where Dame Nature pours profusely bounties
from her goodly store —
There I saw them vigils keeping, shining backs
for ever peeping
From the grass where I beheld them, veritable
ghosts of yore.
And a voice beside me whispered : “From the
festive days of yore
Empty bottles, n ithing more.”
So the cue thus found I followed, as I trod o’er
ground all hollowed
To enrich Chinese who wallowed, where white
men had heretofore
Worked the mines with proper methods, but had
sunk before the slipshods.
What could be the cause? I shouted, “Tell me,
tell me I implore !’’
And the voice beside me echoed, “Tell me, tell
me I implore !
Bottles ! bottles ! Nothing more.’’
Days had come in olden Southport, when the
bottles gave great mouth-sport,
And their drinkers daily stretched out, stretched
them out upon the floor.
Those were days of constant chances —which now
come like firefly glances.
At what work then did they labor, daily labor by
the score,
Those fine miners, at what labor did they labor
by the score ?
Emptying bottles, nothing more.
Then those scenes of sad disaster followed ever
faster, faster—
Idle men, dishonest master, neglected mines of
priceless store —
Blots these on our history’s pages, which will shock
the modern sages,
And the men in coming ages who may read our
records o’er,
When they find just what has happened as they
read our records o’er —
Through the bottles, nothing more.
So N.T. began to languish. Then there came
that flood of anguish—
Chinese from the ports of China surging up upon
our shore :
And with methods old and tardy, they—thesteady,
sober, hardy—
Won the white man’s gold and country, ever,
ever, more and more ;
And our thanks for this is owing, for this ever more
and more—
To the bottles, nothing more.
Should it ever be so fated that white men, by
“ John ” disrated,
Should become all isolated from N.T.’s exten-
sive shore,
And to wipe us from existence “John” main-
tains with great persistence,
This had always been a China —as he read in
books of yore—
What will show we had a being, and disprove
those books of yore ?
Empty bottles ! Nothing more.
The cruel Cootamundra murder appears
likely to be added to the long list of N.S.W. un-
avenged crimes.

The North Australian Pastoral Co., of
Townsville, gives notice that it will prosecute
anybody shooting or otherwise trespassing on
its Woodstock station. The company consists
chiefly of absentees, and its country covers over
1000 sq. miles — i.e., including Inkerman station
and purchased land—and extends from ten miles
north of the Charters Towers railway to within
eight miles of the Bowen line (say over 70 miles
in length, with the exception of a strip of land
under ten miles intervening), with a width in
some places of 30 miles from the coast. The
notice was only advertised once and on the eve of
the open season for shooting.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ TZEIIR-IBIEI CASTLES 77 and “ C-A^ST-A-TsT. 77
We have declined to confoVm with this request, as we feel it would not be to the interest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“ Three Castles” and “ Capstan.” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. (Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

Sydney Pickpockets,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Altogether, there are in Sydney between 40 and
50 professional pickpockets, ranging from 20 to 60
years of age, and recognising in their ranks some
five or six women. Out of the total some two or
three are English; two or three American; the
rest are Australians, mostly Sydney natives. In
the vernacular of the fraternity there are three
chief classes: the “breech-hooks,” numbering
about 20; the “moll-buzzers,” numbering about
12; and the “ bag-dippers,” who vary from 6 to 10.
# • •
The “breech-hook”is the fine and daring gentle-
man of the craft; he flies at big game ;he picks
the pockets of men only, and looks down with
scorn on the lower orders, the “ raoll-buzzers
and “ bag - dippers,” who steal from women.
Doubtless if a richly-dressed woman, who could
easily afford the loss of .£2O, were to tempt the
“ breech-hook ” with her well-lined purse, his
inclination would overcome his sense of honor;
but, in the act of taking it, he would feel a loss of
dignity like that of which a Supreme Court bar-
rister is conscious in pleading a case at a police-
court.
• # *
The “ breech-hook ” reads and writes well, and
has a fair general education. He is well up in
gossiping topics and sporting incidents, and can
make interesting conversation for hours together.
His address is bright and ready ; in his clean
linen and well-tailored suit he looks exactly like a
comfortable bank-clerk.
# • •
He has formed his own code of morals, and his
simple domestic establishment is generally shared
w ith a fashionable member of the demi-monde, whom
he takes to the races and theatres, and«pn whom
he showers the successes of his profession. He
would scorn to take money or even food for
nothing from his mistress ; he never speaks to the
“ moll-buzzer ” or “ bag-dipper,” the low members
of the craft, who disgrace their calling by living at
times upon prostitution.
* * *
Now and again his amour is consecrated with
religious ceremony, and he unites himself J' 1S
mistress by the bonds of holy matrimony. She
gives up the sordid traffic of the person from
which his love has raised her; she studies his pro-
fession; and there are little heartburnings and
troubles when luck is low and his darling wants to
go out “on her own ” to pickpocket, too. _ It is
wife or paramour who sometimes in a jealous
passion betrays him to the wily detective and the
dubious vengeance of the law.
» • #
FromSo’clock in the morning till late atnightthe
“breech-hook” is inthecityplyinghis industry. He
has all his meals at restaurants, and can often be
seen lounging atone or other of them or in one of the
hundred and one popular billiard-rooms. He is as
well-conducted and sober as an evangelist with
money at heart; he is temperate in eating, drink-
ing and smoking. One, when asked about the
much-vexed drink, replied : “ I’m never the worse
for drink, sir, except when I’m down in the world;
I think drunkenness a crime.” It is obvious
enough that the nerve cannot be too steady, nor
the hand too light, that would abstract, unper-
ceived, a roll of bank-notes from the trousers-
pooket of a quick, active business man.
• • •
When misfortunes come in battalions, when the
heart is stricken with the pangs of despised love
or of betrayed friendship, then the “ breech-hook”
seeks solace in the cup.
# # *
He does all the Australian cities in turn, and
with his quick eyes and ears and lively tongue he
gains a wonderful amount of information concern-
ing business-men generally. He will follow' for a
whole day a man whom he suspects of having
money ; he watches the banks week in week out,
selecting his prey and reconnoitring for a haul.
# # •
At work, three sentries travel with oneadept. The
Master hovers near theselected quarry; thedisciples
watch for the detectives, all of whom they know
thoroughly by sight. When the game forces his
way through a knot in the crowd, or devotes his
attention to one of the conspirators having a fit
in the gutter, or when one of the score of oppor-
tunities born of a congested traffic presents itself,
the staffers simultaneously seize the moment, the
wink is given, the adept “ puts his hand down,
and a civilian and his money are abruptly sepa-
rated.
« • •
It is a deft artist who “ puts his hand down,’’
and on account of his wonderful skill and the
sharp vigilance of his mates the detectives have
great difficulty in making captures. The “breech-
hook ” is a stoical thief, and when caught red-
handed he will turn to the detective and remark
with a sturdy resignation, “You’ve got me dead
to rights this time.” And he does his six months
or three years in gaol without halt or whimper.
# * *
The Sydney “ breech-hook ” is a remarkable
craftsman. In his particular line he is the
prince of Australian pickpockets, and, perhaps,
has no rival in the world. Certainly he is far
cleverer than the two or three swell Yankees and
the two or three flash Englishmen who have
wedged themselves into a place in his ranks.
• • •
“Moll-buzzing” is the trade of that cowardly
branch of the profession ilvhieh lives by picking
the pockets of women. Ask a true “ breech-hook”
whether he is “ breech-hooking ” or “moll-
buzzing,” and he will reply with a fine scorn ;
“ Moll-buzzing 1 I’d sooner go ‘ breech-hooking
on my own.” That is, ho would prefer, un-
attended by scouts, to face the greater risk and
difficulty of robbing men.
# # *
The “moll-buzzer” hangs, with a mate or two,
about railway-stations, tram waiting-rooms, large
drapers’ and milliners’ shops, and at every spot
. where women wait for ’buses and trains. Woe
to the woman who gets lost in contemplation of a
handsome bonnet-window, or who allows two men
to jostle her while they assist her and her children
and parcels into ’bus or tram or train. The polite
volunteers are always ready to pay themselves
with her purse.
# # *
The “bag-dippers” are another section who
prey upon women. They travel by threes in
single file, and overtake women carrying bags or
baskets, and going in the same direction. Ihe
first man, in passing, merely opens the bag ; the
second puts his hand in; the third watches and is
ready to give the alarm if necessary.
* * *
Watch-picking, the great art of the English
pickpocket, is seldom practised in Australia. A
few clever watch-pickers came from London to
Australia a few years ago, but could not make a
success of their game. In other lines of the busi-
ness they were behind the local man, and only
managed to live by hobnobbing with him. Cutting
out pockets with scissors is another English trick
little patronised in Australia.
• • «
L ,an English master, came to Australia
four or five years ago, and introduced “ringing
the bags,” which is a favorite dodgeof the Eng-
lish and American thief. Though simple, it has
produced grand results. A clerk who goes regu-
larly, bag in hand, to a bank is marked for plunder.
One day as he is at the bank-counter and is plac-
ing his money in his bag, a well-dressed man says,
with affable alarm, “Sir, excuse me, you have
dropped a <£s-note !” The clerk looks down, sees
the note upon the floor, marvels at his own care-
lessness and the honesty of his benefactor, picks
the note up and puts it into his bag. But the bag
is not his ; it is only an exact duplicate, which the
thieves have left in place of his and the bulk of his
money. L ’s gang worked this trick for
,£3OOO in Adelaide, and made good hauls in Mel-
bourne and Sydney. But two years ago in Sydney
L ’s mate, Belfast, was nabbed with bag
and all at the counter of the Bank of N.S.W.
L bolted to Maoriland, and subse-
quently the rest of his gang, Henry, Miller, and
Long Tom, went with him to America. There
they had marked success until all four were caught
in the act of bank-robbing at Minneapolis and re-
ceived 10 years’ imprisonment. Since “Belfast’s”
capture, no case of “ringing the bags” has
occurred in Australia.
• • *
The English and American trick of “call-out”
was practised twice two years ago on Sydney
jewellers. A flash trap drove up to the door when
only one man was in the shop, and the thieves
called him out to attend to them. They showed
him a handsome watch which was out of order,
and when he went back to his shop they decamped
with the plunder which their mate, had secured
while the shopman’s back was turned.
• • •
There is a Sydney class of petty thief who is
original in his dexterity. He is generally a
juvenile, and, under pretence of selling news-
papers, he thrusts a folded one close to a civilian’s
face and picks the tie-pin he is wearing. These
“prop” thieves, as they call themselves, num-
bered 15 or 16 a few years ago; but several have
been convicted and several frightened, and only
four or five are daring enough to continue practice.
Sydney hasonly half-a-dozen female pickpockets,
and only one of them can be termed a orafts-
woman. She has lived by her fingers for 20
years, and is now, at 50 years of age, one of
the quickest pickpockets known. The rest of the
women thieves belong to the type of which
the notorious S is the best representative.
She is a prostitute of such abnormal vitality that
she has never suffered from the after-effects of a
wound inflicted on her by a jealous lover, which
“would have killed any other woman in the world.”
Her game is to talk and fool with the maudlin
youth in the city streets at night, and lavish en-
dearing caresses upon him which cost him his
money and valuables. Big drapery-establish-
ments are frequented by the female thief, as many
a woman knows who has put her purse aside and
forgotten it for a moment; shop-walkers and chiefs
of these establishments are ever on the alert for
well-known female thieves, who will snatch up
and secrete anything portable. When one such
female thief was caught, and her dress examined,
there were found, attached to hooks on her under-
clothing, no less than seven pairs of boots.
Pocket-picking and shop-thieving in Sydney are
decidedly on the increase. The hardy burglar is
turning to it, for it is almost as lucrative as his
own treacherous calling, while the sentences
awarded for the respective offences are altogether
disproportionate. There is very little satisfaction
in a 10-years’ term for burglary, but there is only
a short break to a busy life in a 6-months’ sentence
for “ breech-hooking.”
Again the absurd anomaly of the Melb.
Good Friday, with the pious ideal realised—pubs,
open, and theatres shut.
Apropos the Chinese oath, the favorite
story of a "Vic. County Court Judge may be rung
in.” They were altogether puzzled how to get at
the religion of a Chinaman, and even a rooster’s
head was cut off in vain. Then the interpreter
held up a lighted match. The witness pulled a
little black pipe out of his pocket, and lighted it
with “ Welly good ! ”
Some items from the English papers for
Feb. last: —
A gamekeeper, watching in a plantation for poachers,
was frozen to death ; an old woman in a flreless room was
found killed by the cold beneath her scanty blankets; a
lad was found with his tongue frozen to an iron railing—
he had ventured to touch it with his tongue and was
instantly frozen. An omnibus-driver was observed to be
on his seat in a half-recumbent position. He was dead
with the reins frozen in his hands. Many deaths were
reported from gangrene of the bowels, caused by the
extreme cold. Five London omnibus-drivers, one con-
ductor, and two cabmen died from the cold. A carter
died on his van at Fulham. A London policeman fell dead
while on his beat. More than one country postman
perished miserably by the roadside. The chairman of
the Wandsworth and Olapham Guardians suddenly ex-
pired on going into the open air. A man died while
waiting tor an omnibus. An army pensioner, who was at
the siege of Sebastopol, died in the street. A man, over-
come by the cold at Tottenham, fell, and when discovered
he was dead, his face beintf frozen to the bottom of a sink.

Marian’s Child .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
First we thought of the river,
But the body might be found;
And it did not seem so cruel
To bury it in the ground.
So small it seemed, so helpless—
I hardened my heart like stone—
She kissed it over and over,
And then I heard her groan.
I took it out of her bosom
It cried, and cried, and cried j
I carried it down the garden—
The moon was bright outside.
I dug a hole with a shovel
And laid the baby down ;
I shovelled the sand upon it—
The sand was soft and brown.
But, ah ! its cry was bitter—
I scarce could cover it in.
And when at last ’twas bidden
I sank beneath my sin.
Down at the foot of the garden,
Where the moon-made shadows fell,
I sold myself to the Devil
And bought a home in hell.
Down at the foot of the garden,
Where the weeds grew rank and wild,
Under the shivering willows
I murdered Marian’s child j
My heart was wildly beating,
My eyes and cheeks were wel^
For I heard the baby crying—
-0 God I I hear it yet.
I hear it crying, crying,
Just as I heard it cry
In Marian’s arms in the morning
When I knew that it must die.
Neither of us was woman—
-1 was the younger one;
And we strove to tell each other
What a wise thing we had done.
Why should it live to plague us?
Why should it ever begin
Travelling roads of trouble,
Soiling its soul with sin ?
Marian ! ah, she remembers I
In spite of all her tears
Sweet children call her mother
These many, many years.
Yet when I saw my darling,
Her blue eyes seemed to swell :
“ Annie !” she said, “ do you hear it ?
Listen ! I hear it well 1
“ In the night I hear it calling
With a muffled, plaintive wail,
And my heart stands still to count its sobs,
And always I try and fail;
For I think the depth of my baby’s grief
Will never fathomed be
Till the fires are lit in the bottomless pit
To blast eternity.”
«
Once in a southern city
Joy came into my life—
He loved me, kissed me, thought me
Worthy to be his wife . . ,
No, I will never marry.
God ! I had rather die—
If ever I had a baby
’Twould curse me with its cry !
For down at the foot of the garden,
Where the moon-made shadows fell,
I sold myself to ‘the Devil
And bought a home in hell.
S.A.
J. S. NEILSON.
The famous “ Tantanoola tiger,” which
appears intermittently in S.A. and Victoria, was
list seen by a fox-hunter, who was badly seared
by “ a large spotted animal with head erect and
cavernous mouth studded with great spiked teeth.”
1 he sight of the beast, however, had a more curious
effect on the dogs, according to the Hamilton
(Vic.) Spectator :
The dogs outpaced the horse at first, but presently they
turned tail and came towards him, giving out frightened
whelps and cowering in their gait with fear.
The tiger which fills the blank left by innumer-
able bunyips and the Dora-Dora blacks, is alleged
to be identical with a cub which escaped from a
travelling menagerie at Mt. Gambier (S.A.) thir-
t en years ago.
A N.S. W. country newspaper man empties
the bottle, runs his fingers through his hair, gazes
fiercely at the loose shingle in the roof, adjusts his
own loose shingle, jabs the pen in with deter-
mination, and splutters :
On Monday two children were drowned. Their gossip-
ing parents went to visit a neighbor, leaving the babies
with a full water-butt open in the house. The pair of
them should be flogged to the sea, and there thrown in
to be devoured by the sharks always on the alert for
carrion. The fact is that thousands of women are
allowed to become wives who are totally unfitted
for the position. Just as some girls will marry
anything in masculine attire, so are there foolish
mm who will lead to the altar any doll who has
a winning way, never giving a thought as to whether the
little humbug can cook a chop or make a pudding.
Mothers are mostly to blame. The woman who allows
her daughter to grow up without teaching her to pro-
perly cook a steak, make a pudding, and iron a shirt is
worse than a criminal. Nearly every man who has gone
to destruction has been sent there by his wife. The
worker goes out for a couple of hours before breakfast to
return at 8 o’clock and have put before him a junk of
meat that has been placed in a pan half-full of dripping.
The wife gets hold of a yellow-back novel and times about
six minutes for one side of the meat to cook, hut if the
chapter be a long one, or particularly interesting,
the time is extended. A sensible woman would
not use a pan at all, or if she did the drip-
ping would not be required. Prying-pans are one of
the abominations of our civilisation. The wife who really
respects her husband would take the steak and beat it for
her life for five minutes with the rolling-pin, to ensure
tenderness, then she would carefully cook it, have a tiot
dish, and put a piece of butter upon the cooked meat
The breakfast would then be easily digested, and the man
built up for the day. In the majority of cases woman is
man’s murderer. We have seen a woman howling over a
departed husband, and at the same time we knew she had
really killed the poor creature by her bad cooking.
The Archimedean liver again !
A Sydney firm of lithographers re-
cently got a post-card from a Vic. country editor,
asking quotation for a picture of “a human
stomach laid open and in the intestines seen a
number of worms writhing and crawling about,
also brain ditto.” It was probably wanted to
illustrate an article on the accursed drink.
Judge Windeyer said, when sentencing
George Dean to death on what the public regards
as insufficient circumstantial evidence, that he
was as certain that the prisoner had administered
poison to his wife as if he had seen the deed with
his own eyes. But, then, as the offensive Crick
remarked in N.S.W. Assembly, you can’t always
believe your eyes—at a Mellon stance, for in-
stance. Cook-sureness is a sore affliction, especially
in a Judge. Look at the Biittner case. Biittner,
a lodginghouse-keeper, a year or two ago in
Sydney, was cast for death on a charge of rape—
allegedly committed on a virtuous girl who had
sought the shelter of his house. The presiding
Judge (Foster) concurred in the verdict, but on
the very day Biittner was to have been hanged he
walked, a free man, into the office of lawyer Crick,
his defender, to thank him for his efforts—prose-
cutrix haying confessed (after the Brisbane police
had identified her as a street-walker, despite the
apparently-conclusive medical evidence adduced
at the trial) thatshe had been a consenting party.
And, after inflicting upon Biittner this great
wrong—after having condemned him and shaved
him and ironed him in the condemned cell, the
State actually refused to give him more than £5O
compensation, and this only on condition that he
left the country 1
The new regulations of the Union s.s. co.
of Maoriland provide—
That first and second officers are to be provided with
charts at the company ’s expense, that the captains’ charts
are to be open to their inspection, and that they are to
right, if they think that the vessel is not keeping 1
to her proper course, to point out the presumed error. If
the captain disregards their representations, they are, it
is stated, to put in a protest in writing, and to report the
matter at the next port of call. The circular also con-
tains regulations as to the sale of drink to officers on
board the steamers. It is provided that, other things
being equal, those officers who are total abstainers will
be preferred in reference to promotion.
And just here a new difficulty turns up. The
Wairarapa smash happened because the captain
refused to listen to the officers’ protests. There-
fore. for a time, there will be a tendency to sit
down hard upon any captain who doesn’t attend
to such protests, and the captain will probably
give vyay on this account. Then by-and-bye some
captain, who is taking the right course on a murky
night, will give way once too often, and the result
will be unutterable ruin, and after that it will be
necessary to fix up some new regulation. Acci-
dents will happen, so long as man is what he is,
eyen if the right of protest is given to everybody,
right down to the stowaway and the cook’s cat.
Dear Bulletin Scotty the Wrinkler”
and other authorities assert that the Australian
doesn’t light. He does, sometimes, and I respect-
fully tender my swollen eye and the broken nose
of a friend as evidence to that effect. The other
Saturday I was at a picnic at a seaside town
about 15 miles from Melbourne. It was not a
larrikin affair, by any means, but an ordinary
picnic of bank-clerks, dudes, nice girls who “do
the block’’in the afternoon, and the like. In the
evening, when the final dance was over, I and
another man were changing our shoes in the
dressing-room when a local youth intruded himself,
and fell over my friend’s feet. Then he bade us
“get out of the way,” and hit out without
further ceremony. My friend, who has no science
whatever, lashed out wildly in response, and left
the youth in a stunned condition on the floor.
Then three more local youths ran in, and the five
of us swiped blindly at each other for a time,
till, hearing the whistle blow, we broke through
them and ran for the train. When we were in the
train, my companion looked over the partition into
the next compartment, and a youth who was there
with two girls took offence thereat, and used
language. Then the pair proceeded to plug each
other across the partition in a wild, whirling
fashion, till the next station was reached. There
were five minutes to spare, so they got out and
battered promiscuously at each other till starting
time, and, just at the last moment, my mate, who
was sawing the air as if his arms were two flails,
happened to land in on the top of his antagonist’s
head and laid him out. The defeated warrior was
left behind in the confusion. After that all
was peace until the changing station was reached,
and there we found two of our picnickers banging
each other unscientifically on the platform, and a
small Hebrew youth called Abrahams cheering
them on. My mate remonstrated in a fatherly
manner and Abrahams rushed him on the spot.
Also Abrahams’ brother Isaac charged at me, and
there was a great conflict of six. It was a beau-
tiful free fight, and the air was full of hats, and
gore, and language, and hair, and fighting dude,
and debris. But, just at the most interesting
point, a louder riot rose outside the station, and
we suspended our disagreement and ran to the
scene. It was the rest of our picnic whic i was
being overpowered by another picnic, so we
r ushed to the rescue. The rest of the proceedings
were too confused for description. All I remember
is that my eye was damaged, and Jones’s nose
became a ruin, and that Abrahams and Isaac
fought desperately, and every dude went
straight into the fray and gave no quarter. No-
body was much hurt, as it happened, but it was a
joyous combat while it lasted, and when it was
over, and the two picnics had been dragged
asunder by the railway-officials, everybody felt
better for the exercise. Yes, the Australian does
fight, sometimes, especially when he is full of tea
and buns and ozone.—Yours, &c., M’G.
A GAIETY GLEE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Welcome, girls, though the gold-top gang
Of a bygone year far and wide are scattered !
Gaiety girls once danced and sang
that gang who grinn’d as if hugely flattered
Midnight suppers and gifts galore
Made Melbourne mad for a bygone bevy,
Now it’s a case of “ Ah, never more ! ”
For the chap in chokey the time hangs heavy.
Welcome, girls! In the old wild days,
The days of dear Nellie, Sylvia, Letty,
The gold-top gang flung fair bouquets
And shed much weep when they left the jetty ;
But those were the days when we didn’t long
With the longing of Need for a tinselled tonio—-
Now there’s a craving for Dance and Song
To lighten a load that is getting chronic.
H. C. COGHLAN.
